201403	482	2014-03-04	confcall	TARULLO	I’ll make a few comments here.	92	9
201403	483	2014-03-04	confcall	TARULLO	First—and I think this picks up on a theme that Dennis introduced, and several people have echoed it since then—I do believe that, particularly as we pass the 6.5 percent threshold, we’re better advised to have somewhat generally stated standards in the FOMC statement, which can be elaborated upon as circumstances dictate.	92	9
201403	484	2014-03-04	confcall	TARULLO	I might deviate a bit from what Dennis stated in expressing a strong bias in favor of having the Chair do most of that elaboration, particularly, though not exclusively, in the press conferences that she’ll be conducting.	92	9
201403	485	2014-03-04	confcall	TARULLO	To the degree that we think of communication of forward guidance as itself an element of monetary policy, I think it becomes particularly important to have an authoritative voice elaborating that position, and there’s no alternative here, really, but the Chair.	92	9
201403	486	2014-03-04	confcall	TARULLO	A second point is, although I’ve got, personally, a fair amount of flexibility on the specific language that we will adopt, consistent with that notion of general standards, I do think it’s important that we not lose the advantage of what we’ve gotten already in the language that we’ve inserted into the existing paragraph 5.	92	9
201403	487	2014-03-04	confcall	TARULLO	One of those things is the notion that we are likely—not certain, but likely—to stay at the zero lower bound well past the time when we pass a 6½ percent unemployment rate.	92	9
201403	488	2014-03-04	confcall	TARULLO	And, second, as we begin to increase interest rates, we will, to use our euphemism, “take a balanced approach.”	92	9
201403	489	2014-03-04	confcall	TARULLO	It might be advisable to substitute in that “balanced approach” language something that makes mention of—and I’m not sure this is the right term—the headwinds that we’ll be confronting, because those give a justification for why we think the path upward is, again, not likely to be steep, although we’re not prejudging anything.	92	9
201403	490	2014-03-04	confcall	TARULLO	I would just note in passing that, although I’ve got a fair amount of flexibility on the language and, in some respects, I like some of the earlier statements in option 2 rather than in option 5, I really am less concerned with that.	92	9
201403	491	2014-03-04	confcall	TARULLO	I would say, though, that I think there’s a bit of a risk here.	92	9
201403	492	2014-03-04	confcall	TARULLO	Not many people have proposed it, but there’s a bit of a risk that too much reliance on projections as our standard becomes a bit circular.	92	9
201403	493	2014-03-04	confcall	TARULLO	That is, it almost is the conclusion being stated as the standard.	92	9
201403	494	2014-03-04	confcall	TARULLO	If you think inflation is going to be below target and employment is going to be below target, and that’s your projection, then, almost by definition, you’re going to be in favor of a longer period of accommodation, and you’re in favor of it because of your expectation that that’s where we’re going to be.	92	9
201403	495	2014-03-04	confcall	TARULLO	So I’m not sure it communicates an enormous amount of information very effectively, but I say that with the understanding that we already use projections and we’re going to continue to use projections as we go forward here and in other parts of the statement.	92	9
201403	496	2014-03-04	confcall	TARULLO	The next point is that what we’ve got now is, first, some indication of when we’ll begin to raise rates and, second, some indication of how steep a climb we’ll have once we begin.	92	9
201403	497	2014-03-04	confcall	TARULLO	That’s where, I think, we don’t want to lose the benefits of the communications we’ve had to date, and we want to keep market expectations about where they are.	92	9
201403	498	2014-03-04	confcall	TARULLO	We haven’t yet said anything about the endpoint, and this is where I, like several of you, find paragraph 6 in a few of the options attractive, because it does state an endpoint.	92	9
201403	499	2014-03-04	confcall	TARULLO	And it seems to me it’s an endpoint that, while not giving a specific number, communicates something about how we’ll be thinking about policy along the way.	92	9
201403	500	2014-03-04	confcall	TARULLO	Richard is quite right that we haven’t had a debate on that, and it may be that it would be premature to adopt it.	92	9
201403	501	2014-03-04	confcall	TARULLO	But, unlike Sandy, I’m a little bit concerned about taking it one step at a time.	92	9
201403	502	2014-03-04	confcall	TARULLO	Let’s just say when we’re going to begin, and sometime later we’ll talk about how steep a climb, and sometime thereafter we’ll talk about the endpoint.	92	9
201403	503	2014-03-04	confcall	TARULLO	I think it’s preferable to say something about all three—again, in somewhat generally stated standards—and then allow the Chair to elaborate after listening to the whole Committee during the quarterly meetings that are followed by press conferences.	92	9
201403	504	2014-03-04	confcall	TARULLO	With respect to the financial-stability point, I think Jim had it just right.	92	9
201403	505	2014-03-04	confcall	TARULLO	It would be useful to make reference to it, but not to make too big a deal of it.	92	9
201403	506	2014-03-04	confcall	TARULLO	And a simple minimalist reference to financial-stability considerations would be just fine, from my point of view, but I don’t feel very strongly about that.	92	9
201403	507	2014-03-04	confcall	TARULLO	In conclusion, Madam Chair, I would stay away, as much as possible, from projections being the basis for the standards.	92	9

201403	512	2014-03-04	confcall	STEIN	Thank you, Madam Chair.	92	9
201403	513	2014-03-04	confcall	STEIN	Let me start with a few premises—three, in particular—and then I’ll comment on the options.	92	9
201403	514	2014-03-04	confcall	STEIN	One, as several others have observed, is that we’re basically in a good place.	92	9
201403	515	2014-03-04	confcall	STEIN	Market expectations are roughly lined up with what I take to be the center of mass of where the Committee sees the appropriate path.	92	9
201403	516	2014-03-04	confcall	STEIN	Like many others, again, I think it’s good not to “overtinker,” to do no harm, and to remember that we’ve got considerable support—beyond what’s in the FOMC statement—for the guidance, both in your credibility on this issue and in the LSAP timing factor, which is that if we just keep the LSAPs going until roughly the end of 2014, it’s hard for people to envision a world in which we lift off before mid- 2015.	92	9
201403	517	2014-03-04	confcall	STEIN	This suggests not feeling the need to overdo it in the statement.	92	9
201403	518	2014-03-04	confcall	STEIN	The second point is, I think we want to begin thinking about all of this in terms of a transition back to a more normal world and asking ourselves how we will want to communicate in that more normal world.	92	9
201403	519	2014-03-04	confcall	STEIN	Then we should ask, is what we’re setting up now going to converge smoothly to that desired end state?	92	9
201403	520	2014-03-04	confcall	STEIN	Or are we setting up a bunch of one-offs, tests regarding certain variables, that are helpful and serve a purpose now but we may have to awkwardly dismantle later on?	92	9
201403	521	2014-03-04	confcall	STEIN	So that’s the second consideration.	92	9
201403	522	2014-03-04	confcall	STEIN	The third thing—and this is something that Narayana pointed out—is that there’s a clear challenge: The collective view is that the right time-consistent rate is below what a naive application of a Taylor rule would seem to suggest.	92	9
201403	523	2014-03-04	confcall	STEIN	We may ultimately converge back to doing something like a Taylor rule, but we need a coherent narrative that both takes us there and, ideally, self-liquidates.	92	9
201403	524	2014-03-04	confcall	STEIN	That is, in the process of getting to where we want to be now, we don’t leave ourselves with a lot of junk DNA in the statement.	92	9
201403	525	2014-03-04	confcall	STEIN	At the same time, we have to have a strong, coherent explanation—especially if the news comes in a little bit good—when somebody says, “Hey, Taylor says you should be doing this.	92	9
201403	526	2014-03-04	confcall	STEIN	Why aren’t you doing it?”	92	9
201403	527	2014-03-04	confcall	STEIN	In this spirit, one observation is the asymmetry induced by the zero lower bound and the fact that, in a world of uncertainty, it’s just time consistent and rational to wait a little bit longer as a result of that uncertainty, because, if you get a little bit behind, you can always raise, but if you raise prematurely, you can’t undo that.	92	9
201403	528	2014-03-04	confcall	STEIN	This is something that’s missing from all of the options, and it’s something, Madam Chair, that you mentioned at the outset, and it surprises me that we’re not taking a little bit more advantage of it.	92	9
201403	529	2014-03-04	confcall	STEIN	I think it’s a nice bit of logic.	92	9
201403	530	2014-03-04	confcall	STEIN	If you lean on it a little bit more, you have to lean less on other special factors and data idiosyncrasies, which may work for now but then may not work later.	92	9
201403	531	2014-03-04	confcall	STEIN	And I take that to be a little bit of a lesson from Carney’s adventure with 7 percent.	92	9
201403	532	2014-03-04	confcall	STEIN	It seemed as though it was going to get you somewhere, and then it didn’t.	92	9
201403	533	2014-03-04	confcall	STEIN	So I like that.	92	9
201403	534	2014-03-04	confcall	STEIN	I like the fact that it’s a self-liquidating thing.	92	9
201403	535	2014-03-04	confcall	STEIN	It has a logic now that just pulls away as we normalize.	92	9
201403	536	2014-03-04	confcall	STEIN	I think one could make more use of this asymmetry.	92	9

201403	607	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	Thank you, Chair Yellen.	92	9
201403	608	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	It is great to have the opportunity to work with you as Chair.	92	9
201403	609	2014-03-04	confcall	YELLEN	Thank you.	92	9
201403	610	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	I want to first start by answering the questions and then I’ll make a few general observations, given the discussion that we’ve had.	92	9
201403	611	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	In terms of the questions, I am very much where most people are.	92	9
201403	612	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	I’d prefer a qualitative approach rather than a quantitative approach.	92	9
201403	613	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	It seems to me that the goal of the statement is to describe our expectations about the outlook and then our likely reaction function not just to that outlook but to other possible outlooks, so people can sort of think along with us.	92	9
201403	614	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	Should we link the liftoff explicitly to the end of asset purchases?	92	9
201403	615	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	I think the answer is yes.	92	9
201403	616	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	There is such a link, and it’s highly unlikely that we are going to start to raise the short-term funds rate before we end asset purchases.	92	9
201403	617	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	So why not be explicit about that?	92	9
201403	618	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	I don’t like thresholds, triggers, or floors.	92	9
201403	619	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	I think they are both too complicated and too specific.	92	9
201403	620	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	They are too complicated in the sense that we then have to articulate why we picked that particular threshold, trigger, or floor; they are too specific in the sense that as the information set changes, we may not want to be governed so specifically by the particular threshold, trigger, or floor that we chose.	92	9
201403	621	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	The world is, obviously, more complicated than that, and we see that in the case of the 6½ percent unemployment rate, and we saw that in the case of the Bank of England’s 7 percent unemployment rate threshold.	92	9
201403	622	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	I particularly don’t like thresholds, triggers, and floors that are tied to forecasts.	92	9
201403	623	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	What happens if the forecasts are wrong?	92	9
201403	624	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	And how does the market react to forecast changes?	92	9
201403	625	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	Say we had inflation on a one- to two-year time horizon of 1.6 percent and then we moved it to 1.9 percent.	92	9
201403	626	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	How would the market respond to that?	92	9
201403	627	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	I also think if we tie our actions to our forecasts, it would make the flawed SEP process even more important and introduce the potential for gaming in terms of SEP submissions where the forecast submissions could actually be used to drive the policy outcomes directly.	92	9
201403	628	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	I think it would be really unfortunate if we got into that sort of situation.	92	9
201403	629	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	I agree that having a sentence referencing financial market developments is welcome, but I strongly prefer financial market developments to financial stability because I think the topic of financial market developments emits two separate issues that are important.	92	9
201403	630	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	The first issue is deviations of financial conditions from what we expect, and the second is financial stability, so it includes two very different concepts.	92	9
201403	631	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	The fact is, if financial conditions are grossly different from what we anticipate, this does have implications for monetary policy, so we need to take that into consideration.	92	9
201403	632	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	I very much prefer providing guidance about the trajectory of interest rates after liftoff, both the gradient and the terminal level of the funds rate, because I think that is an important determinant of financial conditions.	92	9
201403	633	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	Bond yields and equity prices today depend on what is going to happen not just in terms of the time of liftoff, but where short-term rates go over the medium to longer term.	92	9
201403	634	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	Leaving out post-liftoff guidance implies that all that matters is the time you lift off.	92	9
201403	635	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	The other problem, I think, in not including it now is that if we don’t include it now, and liftoff gets closer, then we are going to be in this position of, when do we introduce it?	92	9
201403	636	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	And that is going to be a really big market event.	92	9
201403	637	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	So if we’re going to revamp the statement, now is a perfect time to do it, with a new Chair.	92	9
201403	638	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	Let’s get this into the statement now, because it will make it much easier to keep it in the statement going forward.	92	9
201403	639	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	In general, I think the statement needs to be revamped now for several reasons.	92	9
201403	640	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	The 6½ percent unemployment rate threshold is stale, and I think the timing is right with the new Chair.	92	9
201403	641	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	If we don’t alter it in March, it would just make it a much more significant change if we were to revamp it later.	92	9
201403	642	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	So this is actually a singular opportunity to make meaningful changes to the statement.	92	9
201403	643	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	In terms of what I’m for, I am generally inclined to a more minimalist approach, along the lines of either option 1 or 5.	92	9
201403	644	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	If we went down the route of an option 5, I would want to have two sorts of changes.	92	9
201403	645	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	One, as someone observed, it does seem like it has a threshold in there, and I’m a little uncomfortable with that.	92	9
201403	646	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	And, two, I really do think we need a paragraph 6 to explain what happens to the trajectory of interest rates after liftoff.	92	9
201403	647	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	In terms of other changes, I think President Williams made a suggestion that maybe we should put the punchline up-front, that short-term rates aren’t going to be raised until a considerable time after the end of asset purchases.	92	9
201403	648	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	I like that coming first, rather than burying the lead.	92	9
201403	649	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	I think we should at least consider the idea of being more explicit in the statement by saying that the changes are not designed to shift market expectations.	92	9
201403	650	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	I’m not really sure how to do that.	92	9
201403	651	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	But if you remember, in December 2012 when we shifted to an unemployment rate threshold, we said, “The Committee views these thresholds as consistent with its earlier date- based guidance.”	92	9
201403	652	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	And I thought that was good because it is going to be firmer if you have that in the statement than if you just defer it to the press conference.	92	9
201403	653	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	If you decide not to include it in the statement, I think it is extremely important that it be discussed at the press conference.	92	9
201403	654	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	I think the press conference is extremely important.	92	9
201403	655	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	In addition to making it clear that we are not trying to alter expectations, I think we want to explain in the press conference why the statement needed to be changed, the staleness of the 6½ percent unemployment rate, and the importance of linking the interest rate liftoff to the end of asset purchases because that is how the Committee is thinking about that.	92	9
201403	656	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	I think it’s important to provide guidance.	92	9
201403	657	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	I think we need to explain to people a little bit more that we’re providing long-term guidance on the path of short- term rates because it’s important for financial conditions.	92	9
201403	658	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	I don’t think we have really articulated it to the full degree that we need to.	92	9
201403	659	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	And I think, in the press conference, you could explain a little bit more why financial market developments are important as well.	92	9
201403	660	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	I think Governor Powell made a good suggestion about reinvestments.	92	9
201403	661	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	Our last statement about this in 2011 indicated that we would likely begin the process of normalization by ceasing reinvestments.	92	9
201403	662	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	I feel like that has been hanging out there as something that, you know, clearly doesn’t apply any more.	92	9
201403	663	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	We are not going to want to cease reinvestments six months before liftoff, because we are not going to know when six months before is.	92	9
201403	664	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	So I think we should really try to think about correcting that as well.	92	9
201403	665	2014-03-04	confcall	DUDLEY	Thank you, Madam Chair.	92	9

201403	684	2014-03-19	meeting	YELLEN	Why don’t we start with Simon and the financial developments update?	61	6
201403	685	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	Thank you, Madam Chair.	61	6
201403	686	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	US economic data generally disappointed for much of the intermeeting period and contributed to lower US Treasury yields, as shown in your top-left panel.	61	6
201403	687	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	Many market participants attributed the soft data in part to severe winter weather.	61	6
201403	688	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	Despite the disappointing data, concerns over China, and escalating tensions in Eastern Europe, US equity prices rose notably, as also shown in the top-left panel.	61	6
201403	689	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	In explaining this increase, market participants pointed to a variety of factors.	61	6
201403	690	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	These include some reversal of the sharp declines in risk asset prices in late January, as feared negative spillovers from emerging markets to domestic financial markets did not materialize and US corporate earnings results were solid.	61	6
201403	691	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	Corporate credit spreads also narrowed over the period, and investment-grade and high-yield spreads are near their tightest levels in the past decade, as reflected in the first two rows of the top-right panel.	61	6
201403	692	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	Despite continued increases in equity prices, valuations for the broad stock market do not appear overly stretched, with price-to-earnings ratios for the S&P 500 only modestly above their averages over recent years, also shown in the top-right panel.	61	6
201403	693	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	While valuations for some smaller-cap biotech and Internet firms are more elevated, they remain below levels seen prior to the crisis.	61	6
201403	694	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	Risk asset prices have been supported by the continued accommodative stance of global monetary policy, which keeps funding costs low.	61	6
201403	695	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	In addition to easing financial conditions, this likely encourages investors to reach for higher-yielding returns, at times in difficult-to-track carry trades.	61	6
201403	696	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	Monetary policymakers have kept nominal interest rates low in major developed economies to achieve their policy objectives, though success has varied.	61	6
201403	697	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	Indeed, as reflected in the middle-left panel, three-year inflation compensation remains below inflation targets in both Japan and the euro area; notably, at this tenor inflation compensation is now substantially higher in Japan than in the euro area.	61	6
201403	698	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	Many market participants still expect the Bank of Japan to take further action to ease monetary policy, though not until after it evaluates the impact of the consumption tax increase.	61	6
201403	699	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	In the euro area, the ECB staff’s March forecast of headline inflation rises from its current level of 0.7 percent to 1.7 percent by the end of 2016.	61	6
201403	700	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	Market participants believe that if inflation does not begin to increase as anticipated, the ECB will take further action.	61	6
201403	701	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	Actual and expected monetary policy tightening by some central banks appeared to support the reduction in emerging market stresses.	61	6
201403	702	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	Tightening actions in Turkey, Brazil, and South Africa were seen as positive steps toward addressing imbalances.	61	6
201403	703	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	These actions resulted in higher short-term interest rates, as shown in the middle-right panel.	61	6
201403	704	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	Furthermore, measures to reduce fiscal accommodation in some emerging markets helped to increase policy credibility by reinforcing perceptions of the authorities’ commitment to a timely correction of imbalances.	61	6
201403	705	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	Despite these positive steps, market participants remain focused on structural vulnerabilities and risks across emerging market economies.	61	6
201403	706	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	Elsewhere in emerging markets, focus has been on developments in Ukraine and their potential impact on global financial markets.	61	6
201403	707	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	While escalating tensions in the region have led to some limited financial market volatility, the impact has been more pronounced on Russian asset prices.	61	6
201403	708	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	To contain pressures on the ruble, the Russian central bank increased its policy rate by 150 basis points in early March—leading to the increase in Russia’s one-year swap rate seen in the middle-right panel—and has been intervening directly in the foreign exchange market.	61	6
201403	709	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	In China, official-sector actions and concerns over a potential moderation in the growth outlook received considerable attention over the period.	61	6
201403	710	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	The bottom-left panel shows the relatively sharp depreciation of onshore renminbi, which followed a depreciation of the official exchange rate fix and reported intervention by the PBOC.	61	6
201403	711	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	Although the actions to depreciate the renminbi may be consistent with concerns over recent moderation in the Chinese growth outlook, market participants generally interpret them as reflecting a new effort to stem rising capital inflows, which have contributed to credit growth beyond levels Chinese policymakers appear comfortable with.	61	6
201403	712	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	In particular, the depreciation likely reflects a desire by the PBOC to introduce greater two- way exchange rate risk after a reported buildup of speculative positions in the offshore market and may have been a precursor for the doubling to 2 percent of the onshore renminbi intraday trading band that was announced this past weekend.	61	6
201403	713	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	Market participants believe that the interventions were at least partially unsterilized and led to the decline in onshore interbank lending rates shown in the bottom-left panel.	61	6
201403	714	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	Chinese credit markets were also in focus as authorities allowed the first domestic bond default since 1997.	61	6
201403	715	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	Participants interpreted the lack of a support package from authorities as signaling a greater willingness to allow for a more market-determined financial system and to reduce implicit guarantees associated with investment products and corporate bonds.	61	6
201403	716	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	While the reaction in Chinese corporate credit markets was limited, copper and iron ore prices declined sharply, as shown in the bottom-right panel.	61	6
201403	717	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	The declines were due in part to concerns of higher future credit costs that could constrain Chinese growth and were amplified by weaker-than-expected Chinese production and trade data.	61	6
201403	718	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	Steve will discuss revisions to the staff’s forecast for Chinese GDP growth in his briefing.	61	6
201403	719	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	In addition, base metals are widely used in China as a financing vehicle for onshore carry trades that invest in corporate credit or other high-yielding assets.	61	6
201403	720	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	Market participants believe that this type of trade is only one of many carry trade strategies present in the Chinese onshore and offshore financial system produced by investors’ attempts to evade regulation and exploit large yield differentials.	61	6
201403	721	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	The top-left panel of your next exhibit reviews the market reaction to the Bank of England’s changes to its forward guidance in mid-February.	61	6
201403	722	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	The BOE shifted emphasis from its 7 percent unemployment threshold to a focus on a broader set of economic indicators.	61	6
201403	723	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	While market participants had generally expected such a shift, following the announcement short-term interest rates rose sharply and broader financial conditions tightened modestly.	61	6
201403	724	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	Market participants attributed this reaction to the MPC’s higher- than-expected GDP projections, which accompanied the change to the guidance, as well as to Governor Carney’s emphasis on these projections in the related press conference.	61	6
201403	725	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	The BOE experience may illustrate the heightened sensitivity of market prices to central bank communications about the outlook as quantitative forward guidance is removed.	61	6
201403	726	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	With regard to expectations of FOMC policy, as shown in the top-right panel, respondents to the Desk’s dealer and buy-side surveys assign high odds to the Committee changing its forward rate guidance, with the vast majority expecting a change to be announced at this meeting.	61	6
201403	727	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	Dealers and buy-side participants assign the highest probability to the Committee providing additional qualitative guidance relevant to determining the appropriate timing of the first rate hike.	61	6
201403	728	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	They also assign a significant probability to the Committee providing additional information on the path of the target rate after the first hike.	61	6
201403	729	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	The median expectation for the timing of liftoff in both surveys remains the second half of 2015—broadly in line with market-based measures—although the median dealer expectation shifted toward a slightly earlier liftoff relative to the January survey.	61	6
201403	730	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	With regard to economic conditions anticipated at liftoff, a new survey question asked about expectations for inflation in the one to two years following the first hike.	61	6
201403	731	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	As shown in the middle-left panel, averaging across respondents, both dealers and buy-side participants assign a probability of around 40 percent to inflation over this horizon registering within 1 quarter point of the Committee’s 2 percent objective.	61	6
201403	732	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	The average probability distribution for each group of respondents appears to be roughly symmetric around 2 percent.	61	6

201403	742	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	In the most recent survey, the decline in the mean was the largest since the question was introduced in mid-2012, and the median response also changed for the first time, declining from 4.0 percent to 3.75 percent.	61	6

201403	744	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	Separate from the survey, market commentary last week highlighted some expectations that Committee participants would revise lower their longer-run target rate forecasts in the SEP.	61	6
201403	745	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	Turning to Desk operations, if the Committee continues reducing the pace of purchases, the Desk plans some operational changes.	61	6
201403	746	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	In particular, we plan to adjust the number of Treasury operations if the pace declines below $30 billion while maintaining the current maturity distribution of purchases, shown in the top-left panel of your third exhibit.	61	6
201403	747	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	This approach will maintain the average duration of Treasury purchases at about nine years.	61	6
201403	748	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	The Committee could, of course, consider alternative approaches as it reduces the pace of purchases, including those that would change the maturity distribution and, thus, the duration target of the Treasury purchases.	61	6
201403	749	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	For MBS, the Desk intends to decrease the size of daily operations to meet the Committee’s monthly purchase pace, though actual daily operation sizes will vary based on fluctuations in monthly reinvestments.	61	6
201403	750	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	We will continue to focus purchases in production coupons.	61	6
201403	751	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	Additionally, as noted in the memo we circulated ahead of the meeting, we are beginning the process of transitioning MBS purchases to our proprietary FedTrade platform, which will improve the efficiency and transparency of our operations.	61	6
201403	752	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	We anticipate that these operations with FedTrade will begin on April 9.	61	6
201403	753	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	Expectations for the path of remaining purchases from the dealer survey imply that the par value of the SOMA portfolio would peak near the end of this year at $4.2 trillion.	61	6
201403	754	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	At that point, SOMA holdings would consist of $2.5 trillion in Treasuries and $1.7 trillion in MBS, as shown in the top-right panel.	61	6

201403	756	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	Increases in the size and duration of SOMA holdings have resulted in a substantial rise in the interest rate risk of the portfolio, as was the policy intent in removing duration from the market.	61	6

201403	761	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	Turning to the overnight RRP exercise, with approval from the Chair, the rate and the allotment cap were increased over the intermeeting period.	61	6
201403	762	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	As seen in the bottom-left panel, market repo rates increased over the period, along with Treasury bill yields and the effective federal funds rate (not shown).	61	6
201403	763	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	According to market participants, these increases were likely in response to a pickup in Treasury bill issuance related to the reinstatement of the debt limit as well as to the higher rate offered in our operations.	61	6
201403	764	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	As illustrated in the bottom-right panel, the majority of Treasury triparty repo transactions have printed within 1 basis point of the operation rate.	61	6
201403	765	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	The anticipated seasonal decline in bill issuance in April is expected to push market rates lower and thus may provide another opportunity to further examine the degree to which the RRP operations influence market repo rates.	61	6
201403	766	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	The take-up in the operations continues to respond primarily to the spread between the rate offered and the market rate for overnight repo.	61	6
201403	767	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	As shown in the top-left panel of your final exhibit, when this spread is near zero, take-up appears to be highly elastic; this is particularly true for government money market funds and primary dealers.	61	6
201403	768	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	Take-up in the operations averaged around $89 billion during the intermeeting period, and the operations may be having some effect on volumes in other money markets.	61	6
201403	769	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	In particular, as shown in the top-right panel, since the start of the exercise, triparty repo volumes have decreased alongside the increase in take-up.	61	6
201403	770	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	Brokered fed funds volumes have recently ticked lower, as shown in the middle-left panel, perhaps partly because of the participation in the exercise by the four FHLB counterparties.	61	6
201403	771	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	Overall, we haven’t learned anything yet that suggests overnight RRPs could not be a powerful addition to our toolkit for the control of short-term interest rates.	61	6
201403	772	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	That said, there are additional enhancements we could make to further test the efficacy of the operations.	61	6
201403	773	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	Additionally, the seven-day term deposit operations carried out over the period went smoothly.	61	6
201403	774	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	The staff plans to outline testing options for the Committee in April in combination with further discussion of the intermediate and long-run framework options.	61	6
201403	775	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	A quick update about the dollar swap lines:  As shown in the middle-right panel, their usage has declined to very low levels.	61	6
201403	776	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	Recall that in January I sent a memo to the Committee informing you of the contingent plans of the BOE, ECB, SNB, and BOJ to discontinue regular three-month and seven-day operations.	61	6
201403	777	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	We expect little market reaction to the phasing-out of these operations after July, as the standing swap lines provide a framework for reintroducing these operations if market conditions warrant.	61	6
201403	778	2014-03-19	meeting	POTTER	A final note:  If the Committee decides to reduce the pace of purchases at this meeting, we will release a Desk statement at the same time as the Committee statement—the same approach as that taken at the two prior meetings.	61	6

201403	934	2014-03-19	meeting	YELLEN	Let’s then turn to our briefings on the economic situation, and David Wilcox will start us off.	11	1
201403	935	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	Thank you, Madam Chair.	11	1
201403	936	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	I’ll be referring to the packet that is creatively titled “Material for Forecast Summary.”	11	1
201403	937	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	As shown in the top-left panel by the gap between the red and black lines, the indicators that have been published since the January Tealbook caused us to revise down our near-term forecast for GDP growth in the March Tealbook by a sizable amount.	11	1
201403	938	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	As you can see in the table to the right by comparing the first two lines, the indicators that were published late last week rearranged the quarterly pattern of GDP growth a little further but left the overall profile of activity broadly unrevised.	11	1
201403	939	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	This morning’s report on housing starts and permits was a little weaker than we had expected and would take another tenth out of GDP growth in each of the first and second quarters of this year relative to what’s shown in your exhibit.	11	1
201403	940	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	Meanwhile, in the labor market, payroll employment growth has been roughly in line with our expectations in the January Tealbook after taking account as best we can of likely weather effects, but once again the unemployment rate has come in a little lower than we expected.	11	1
201403	941	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	Finally, there has been essentially no news on the inflation front.	11	1
201403	942	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	As you know from the Tealbook, we spent a great deal of effort this past round trying to discern how much of the downward surprise to our near-term GDP estimates could be traced to the unusually severe weather that several of you experienced firsthand this winter.	11	1
201403	943	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	(As one piece of quantitative evidence, the “W” word appears 60 times in Tealbook A, 25 times in the Beige Book’s national summary, and—at last count—15 times in this briefing alone.	11	1
201403	944	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	I got tired of counting after that.) Our Tealbook assessment was heavily informed by an extensive empirical investigation undertaken by Charlie Gilbert, a member of our Industrial Output section, which is summarized in a box in the Tealbook.	11	1
201403	945	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	For this exercise, Charlie used daily data from roughly a thousand individual NOAA weather stations to construct measures of extreme weather events at the county level; he then aggregated those readings to the national level using customized activity-related weights and used the resulting measures to explain the behavior of four key monthly economic indicators.	11	1
201403	946	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	With these highly detailed metrics, Charlie was able to detect noticeable effects from both cold and snow on housing starts and auto sales and some restraint of extreme cold on retail sales as well.	11	1
201403	947	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	Payroll employment seems less sensitive to snow or cold, though the average length of the workweek seems more so.	11	1
201403	948	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	Adding up all the pieces, and taking account of the possibility that some of the weather-related hit to final purchases was likely buffered by inventories, our best guess—as shown on line 5 of the table—is that bad weather may have held down GDP growth this quarter by about four-tenths of a percentage point; next quarter, we expect spending and production to rebound, with no net effect on the level of output after the third quarter.	11	1
201403	949	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	We also think that adverse weather probably had little effect on the unemployment rate and only a modest effect on payroll employment.	11	1
201403	950	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	Some outside analysts have circulated estimates much larger than ours.	11	1
201403	951	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	While we would be the first to acknowledge that a good deal of uncertainty surrounds any assessment of weather effects, including ours, we are skeptical that recent weather effects could have been as large as some of the more adventuresome estimates would suggest.	11	1
201403	952	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	Our skepticism is born of issues of timing, location, and sector: Some of the unexpected softness in spending predates the period of bad weather; some occurred in areas of the country that did not have bad weather; and some occurred in categories of spending that probably are not very sensitive to bad weather.	11	1
201403	953	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	Thus, in our estimation, even adjusting for weather effects, the recent data point to a somewhat weaker profile of GDP growth than we had expected in January.	11	1
201403	954	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	As is likely more firmly emblazoned in my memory than yours, a rush of stronger data a few short months ago had encouraged us to mark up our GDP projection in the January Tealbook.	11	1
201403	955	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	It now appears that some of this optimism was misplaced.	11	1
201403	956	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	Thus, it is useful to go one step further back and compare the March forecast to the one we made in December, as I have done in the box in the middle-right panel.	11	1
201403	957	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	The first two lines in the box compare our forecasts of GDP growth over the four quarters ending in the second quarter of this year—a period over which weather effects should come close to washing out; our assessment of GDP growth over that period is essentially unrevised relative to the December Tealbook.	11	1
201403	958	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	Nonetheless, as can be seen by comparing lines 3 and 4, the unemployment rate appears on track to come in three- tenths below our December Tealbook projection.	11	1
201403	959	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	As I highlighted in my January presentation, this situation—a downside surprise in the unemployment rate despite real GDP growth about in line with our forecast— has been present to a greater or lesser degree for the past couple of years.	11	1
201403	960	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	In part, we have responded to these forecast errors by trimming our assumption for the growth rate of potential GDP, and we took a further step along these lines in the most recent forecast.	11	1
201403	961	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	Separately and additionally, this time around we took potential GDP growth in 2015 and 2016 down a touch further to reflect our revised—but still provisional— assessment of the effect that the Affordable Care Act may have on labor supply.	11	1
201403	962	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	The bottom-left panel shows the net effect of these latest revisions on the GDP gap.	11	1
201403	963	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	By the end of the near term, the output gap is about unchanged relative to our January projection.	11	1
201403	964	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	Over the medium term, the revisions that we made to potential output growth pass through one-for-one to our projection for actual output; as we made only minor changes to our other conditioning factors—the only one worth noting being a slightly higher path for the dollar—the projected output gap from the middle of this year onward is little revised relative to January.	11	1
201403	965	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	Our unemployment rate forecast is similarly essentially unrevised relative to January.	11	1
201403	966	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	In the past, we have emphasized that we would need to see a pickup in GDP growth in order to be assured that labor market conditions would continue to improve.	11	1
201403	967	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	With the changes to our supply-side assumptions that we have made over the past few rounds, even continued growth along the lines of what we saw, on average, during 2013 would eventually be sufficient to achieve the employment-related goal in the Committee’s dual mandate.	11	1
201403	968	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	It wouldn’t be speedy, but it would ultimately get the job done.	11	1
201403	969	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	Turning to the other component of the dual mandate, the incoming data on core PCE inflation—shown in the bottom-right panel—have been close to our expectations.	11	1
201403	970	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	After the Tealbook was closed, we received the February PPI and—this morning—the February CPI; in terms of their implications for our PCE projections, these releases were also close to our expectations.	11	1
201403	971	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	With slack, inflation expectations, and core import prices all little revised from our January forecast, we have made essentially no change to our core inflation forecast; likewise, our projection for headline inflation (not shown) is also mostly unrevised over the medium term.	11	1
201403	972	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	We therefore continue to expect a modest pickup in inflation over the projection period— though at this point, such a pickup is completely prospective.	11	1
201403	973	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	Indeed, the anticipated increase is modest enough—especially relative to the usual volatility of the data—that I expect it will be difficult to discriminate in real time as to whether we are seeing the slow attainment of the Committee’s 2 percent target, or whether inflation is actually anchored at some slightly lower level.	11	1
201403	974	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	Steve Kamin will now continue our projection.	11	1
201403	975	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	Several weeks ago, I accompanied Chair Yellen to the meetings of the G-20 and the BIS held in Sydney, Australia.	11	1
201403	976	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	Although 20 hours of flying is an awfully long way to go for some meetings, these discussions proved unusually productive.	11	1
201403	977	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	In their lead-up, the media was rife with comments that the Federal Reserve was oblivious to events beyond our borders, that its tapering had contributed to the eruption of financial stresses earlier this year, and that once normalization of monetary policy began in earnest, it would substantially disrupt emerging financial markets.	11	1
201403	978	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	In Sydney, Chair Yellen convincingly pushed back against these assertions, emphasizing the weight that the Committee places on global economic developments and underscoring how, by supporting US economic growth and by clearly communicating its monetary policy intentions, the Federal Reserve will enact policy that should also redound to the benefit of our trading partners, including the emerging market economies (or EMEs).	11	1
201403	979	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	Indeed, that view underpins our staff projection, in which the global economy converges toward normality by the end of the forecast period.	11	1
201403	980	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	In the advanced foreign economies, real GDP growth runs at 2 percent this year, about the same as last year, and rises to 2¼ percent in 2015 and 2016 as the euro-area economy picks up steam.	11	1
201403	981	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	While hardly a boom, this expansion is sufficient to reduce the amount of slack in these economies to below 1 percent of GDP by the end of the forecast period.	11	1
201403	982	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	The emerging market economies, broadly speaking, are already in the neighborhood of full capacity.	11	1
201403	983	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	In both the previous and current quarters, the pace of expansion in the EMEs looks to have been rather subdued at about 3½ percent, nearly a percentage point below its estimated trend pace, as China and Latin America have shown surprising weakness.	11	1
201403	984	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	However, with some bounceback in these economies, we are projecting EME growth to strengthen to roughly 4½ percent in 2015 and 2016.	11	1
201403	985	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	The narrowing of output gaps in the advanced economies, along with the return of inflation back toward targets—which are generally at 2 percent—will naturally imply some normalization of monetary policies.	11	1
201403	986	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	Among the major foreign central banks, we expect the Bank of England to be the first out of the gate, as UK GDP growth has been brisk and the unemployment rate has fallen nearly to the BOE’s 7 percent threshold.	11	1
201403	987	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	In consequence, the BOE has already announced new forward guidance, as Simon has described, and we see it lifting the policy rate starting early next year.	11	1
201403	988	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	The euro-area economy has been weaker and inflation has been lower than in Britain, so we do not see the ECB raising rates until 2016, and it might even loosen further if the recovery flags.	11	1
201403	989	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	Finally, the BOJ is still in the process of easing policy via asset purchases, and, in fact, we expect it to expand that program sometime soon; however, in our projection, the BOJ completes these purchases in 2015 and implements its first rate hike at the end of 2016.	11	1
201403	990	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	As I noted earlier, during our meetings in Sydney, Chair Yellen and I frequently heard concerns that once monetary policies in the advanced economies began to tighten, this could be quite disruptive for EMEs.	11	1
201403	991	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	In the staff outlook, with the Fed and other central banks communicating their policy intentions as clearly as possible, markets are expected to anticipate the rate hikes so that once they occur, they should not exert large and sustained effects on yields, exchange rates, and other asset prices.	11	1
201403	992	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	In this regard, we take some comfort from the relatively tranquil introduction of tapering last December.	11	1
201403	993	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	Of course, the events of the past year underscore how difficult it is to foretell market responses to our policies, and there may well be segments of the markets that are surprised by even the most clearly telegraphed policy actions.	11	1
201403	994	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	But our sense is that, as long as the EMEs are growing solidly—in part because of the recovery in the advanced economies—and as long as the most vulnerable EMEs are making progress toward better fundamentals, they should be able to weather the market volatility that might arise around the time of policy tightening.	11	1
201403	995	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	However, if at the time we start raising rates, EMEs are struggling to maintain economic growth and continue to face concerns about their desirability as an investment destination, the risks are considerably higher that our policy normalization could have adverse spillovers abroad, a scenario we explored in the Tealbook.	11	1
201403	996	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	In light of this concern, a number of developments have been quite worrisome.	11	1
201403	997	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	First, many Latin American economies have been struggling over the past year and, although we are projecting improved performance down the road, the most recent data have not given us much confidence.	11	1
201403	998	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	In Mexico, despite the pickup in US growth, GDP expanded at only a ¾ percent rate in the first three quarters of last year and stayed at that anemic pace in the fourth.	11	1
201403	999	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	A number of factors have been cited for the recent weakness—auto plants that have been shut down for retooling, fiscal policy that has been less expansionary than expected, and confidence that has been damaged by uncertainty over recent tax hikes—but a solidly grounded expansion should have been more resilient to such shocks.	11	1
201403	1000	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	In Brazil, where the economy appears set to flatten out yet again in the current quarter after a very weak 2013, we see more fundamental reasons for a slump:  sharp hikes in policy rates to contain high inflation, structural bottlenecks that are depressing investment and productivity, and the drag from even worse performance in its neighbors, Argentina and Venezuela.	11	1
201403	1001	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	We are anticipating that Mexico and Brazil will shake off their malaise by next year, but if they are still struggling when the Fed and other advanced economies start raising rates, this could weigh heavily on investor sentiment and significantly worsen their economic and financial difficulties.	11	1
201403	1002	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	A second and even more worrisome risk that could intensify any spillovers from policy normalization, and which would be injurious to many economies in its own right, is a substantially sharper slowdown in growth in China than we are currently projecting.	11	1
201403	1003	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	Based on very weak data for January and February released after the Tealbook was finalized, we now estimate that Chinese GDP growth dropped from 8 percent in the fourth quarter to 6¼ percent in the first, well below our January projection.	11	1
201403	1004	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	Our best guess is that the measures the authorities took last year to push up interest rates and rein in lending, especially in the shadow banking sector, led to a greater pullback in spending than we had expected.	11	1
201403	1005	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	With the People’s Bank of China having allowed interest rates to fall back more recently, and also having engineered a depreciation of the currency, we are projecting growth to bounce back from its current low.	11	1
201403	1006	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	However, after reaching 7¼ percent next quarter, our forecast for Chinese growth edges back down as potential output decelerates and the economy rebalances away from investment and net exports.	11	1
201403	1007	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	We had already marked down our medium-term outlook for China a touch for the March Tealbook, and the most recent data leave us less confident still about our projection.	11	1
201403	1008	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	A sharper slowing of the Chinese economy would by itself weigh on global commodity and financial markets, creating problems for many EMEs.	11	1
201403	1009	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	It would be even more injurious if it triggered problems in the Chinese property and financial sectors that, in turn, caused a much deeper downturn.	11	1
201403	1010	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	At this point, I should probably remind you that our baseline forecast for the global economy is reasonably benign.	11	1
201403	1011	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	Moreover, I can even think of some upside risks to the outlook, such as faster growth in the euro area than the tepid recovery we are predicting.	11	1
201403	1012	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	But I would be remiss if I did not mention Ukraine.	11	1
201403	1013	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	With its economy contracting sharply and the government running out of cash, Ukraine is in very dire straits.	11	1
201403	1014	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	Even if Ukraine succeeds in getting an IMF program, fulfilling program conditions and avoiding default on its obligations will be challenging.	11	1
201403	1015	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	As long as its problems stay mainly in the local economic and political sphere, including the spillovers to Russia that are already taking place, effects on the US and global economy should be limited.	11	1
201403	1016	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	However, if the confrontation between Russia and the West intensifies and leads to global financial market disruptions, the risks to the global outlook become considerably more pronounced.	11	1

201403	1056	2014-03-19	meeting	YELLEN	President Rosengren.	12	1
201403	1057	2014-03-19	meeting	ROSENGREN	I apologize for my inquisitiveness today, but I have two questions.	12	1
201403	1058	2014-03-19	meeting	ROSENGREN	The first one is for David.	12	1
201403	1059	2014-03-19	meeting	ROSENGREN	Your memo on the weather effect, line 5, basically says that it washes out over the course of the year, which was basically my intuition.	12	1
201403	1060	2014-03-19	meeting	ROSENGREN	So if people look at our SEP median forecast and see real GDP is three-tenths lower in 2015 and four-tenths lower in 2016, do you think they will assume that weather had no effect on that because it would have washed out over the course of the year?	12	1
201403	1061	2014-03-19	meeting	ROSENGREN	As you think about private-sector forecasts and your own forecast, do you think it’s a standard assumption that it will all wash out over the course of a year?	12	1
201403	1062	2014-03-19	meeting	ROSENGREN	My second question actually goes to the alternative scenario about emerging markets in the Risks and Uncertainty section in the Tealbook A.	12	1
201403	1063	2014-03-19	meeting	ROSENGREN	You have the financial crisis in the EMEs, and the result is a leveling-off of the unemployment rate at 6½ percent.	12	1
201403	1064	2014-03-19	meeting	ROSENGREN	What’s interesting about this one stress is that it’s different from the others, which are getting more at plausible differences that people might have in their baseline forecasts.	12	1
201403	1065	2014-03-19	meeting	ROSENGREN	This is getting a little bit more at a financial-stability type of stress, for which I commend you.	12	1
201403	1066	2014-03-19	meeting	ROSENGREN	I think it’s a good one to be thinking about.	12	1
201403	1067	2014-03-19	meeting	ROSENGREN	But I do wonder if some of the financial spillovers are incorporated in the modeling that you’re doing.	12	1
201403	1068	2014-03-19	meeting	ROSENGREN	For example, European banks have a much larger exposure to emerging markets than US banks.	12	1
201403	1069	2014-03-19	meeting	ROSENGREN	And so if they are having a stress test in Europe at the same time that this emerging market financial crisis occurs, would you see more of a flow coming through Europe?	12	1
201403	1070	2014-03-19	meeting	ROSENGREN	Would you pick up those secondary factors in the way that you’re doing the modeling?	12	1
201403	1071	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	I’ll go first.	12	1
201403	1072	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	In regard to the EME crisis simulation, we incorporate some types of financial effects on the US economy, but we don’t incorporate others.	12	1
201403	1073	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	Aside from the strictly macroeconomic effects that we incorporate—which are a bunch of shocks to the EMEs themselves that lower their output as well as flight-to-safety shocks that push up the dollar, both of which reduce our trade balance and have a direct impact on US GDP—we do also incorporate a number of financial market shocks in the United States itself, specifically upward shocks to corporate bond spreads and to equity premiums that are in part offset by downward shocks to Treasury yields as part of that flight to safety.	12	1
201403	1074	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	So those are the factors that we do build in, and they are factors that our DSGE model can accommodate.	12	1
201403	1075	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	You mentioned an additional factor, which is the possibility that because European banks are very exposed to emerging markets, and because US banks of course are very exposed to Europe, that could be an additional channel of spillover and contagion, and we have definitely thought of that.	12	1
201403	1076	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	In the QS surveillance report on financial stability that we prepared in December, there is actually some discussion of that, including a table that directly shows that certain economies, particularly the United Kingdom, Switzerland, and Spain, have pretty heavy exposures to the five fragile EMEs, and that US banks in turn have pretty heavy exposure to the United Kingdom, less to the others.	12	1
201403	1077	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	So that’s a plausible channel of contagion.	12	1
201403	1078	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	It is not one that we have built directly into our simulation, but we could think about doing that.	12	1
201403	1079	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	I would note, additionally, that if you think about these different channels, the United Kingdom, for example, has somewhere in the neighborhood of 60 percent of capital as exposure to EMEs, and then US banks have something on the order of 10 percent of Tier 1 capital in exposure to UK banks.	12	1
201403	1080	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	Probably that type of channel alone might not be enough to give you too much bang for the buck in terms of an effect of an EME crisis on US banks, although it would be material.	12	1
201403	1081	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	However, noting that the euro-area economy more generally is pretty fragile, and knowing that the situation of European banks in general is fragile and dependent on the outcome for the euro-area economy, if a crisis in EMEs derailed the euro-area recovery, and if it led to the types of fears of meltdown that were so prevalent a couple of years ago, then that could truly be an avenue for deep contagion through Europe back to the States.	12	1
201403	1082	2014-03-19	meeting	WILCOX	I think the answer to your question about weather-related effects is “yes,” in the sense that I think they should wash out within the calendar year 2014.	12	1

201403	1133	2014-03-19	meeting	WASCHER	So when there was the big influx of teenagers into the labor market in the 1970s, that drove up the natural rate because they tend to have high unemployment rates on average or to change jobs a lot and have periods of unemployment in between.	12	1
201403	1134	2014-03-19	meeting	WASCHER	As we saw the aging of the population, the natural rate looked like it was coming down.	12	1
201403	1135	2014-03-19	meeting	WASCHER	In the mid- 2000s, or 2007, say, we thought the natural rate had come down to about 5 percent, and that seemed consistent with what we were seeing in terms of inflation at the time.	12	1
201403	1136	2014-03-19	meeting	WASCHER	The demographics are a relatively neutral factor since then, and so we have it basically going back down to where it was prior to the financial crisis by the end of the medium-term forecast period.	12	1

201403	1245	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	And in that context, the impact on the economic expansion, particularly in Europe, could be quite damaging.	12	1
201403	1246	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	So that’s the Ukraine part.	12	1
201403	1247	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	On China, the scenario that we worry about is that some combination of slower growth in China interacts with the financial fragilities there—the property market, the shadow banking market, the tremendous amount of dodgy loans—and that those two combine to lead to a financial crisis that pushes growth down a lot further.	12	1
201403	1248	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	So then what’s the impact on the global economy and the United States?	12	1
201403	1249	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	It’s pretty hard to tell.	12	1
201403	1250	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	I will note that in the fall we did a simulation in the Tealbook that looked at a China hard landing.	12	1
201403	1251	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	In that scenario, Chinese growth fell to something in the neighborhood of 5 percent, which is considered quite low by historical standards, and aggregate foreign GDP growth ended up coming in some 3 percent below baseline.	12	1
201403	1252	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	That pushed US GDP about 1 percent or so below baseline, which actually seems moderately contained.	12	1
201403	1253	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	I am thinking as I review that simulation that it might not have given enough weight to financial factors that might attend those developments.	12	1
201403	1254	2014-03-19	meeting	KAMIN	I actually think that if Chinese growth fell to 5 percent or below, it would create downdrafts in global financial markets that would lead to further downward pressures on GDP in China, in EMEs, in foreign advanced economies, and in the United States that would make injury greater than the 1 percent in that simulation.	12	1

201403	1279	2014-03-19	meeting	WILLIAMS	As many in the popular press have suggested, slackening demand from institutional investors appears responsible for some of the decline in home sales.	13	1

201403	1296	2014-03-19	meeting	WILLIAMS	Just to be clear, this is based on CPI data, and we put a 0.3 percentage point gap between rates of PCE and CPI inflation, so we were looking at a 2.3 percent CPI.	13	1
201403	1297	2014-03-19	meeting	WILLIAMS	They find that the probability that inflation will be in this target range has almost doubled over the past year, moving up from about 20 percent to 40 percent, and financial market participants are putting money on the table that both the upside and the downside tail risks to the inflation outlook are lower than they’ve been in recent years.	13	1

201403	1357	2014-03-19	meeting	ROSENGREN	I have the economy growing for the rest of this year at roughly 3 percent and then growing slightly faster in 2015.	13	1
201403	1358	2014-03-19	meeting	ROSENGREN	This growth is sufficient to return the economy to my estimate of the natural rate of unemployment of 5¼ percent by the end of 2016, which, if correct, means that it will have taken nine years to reach full employment from the beginning of the recession in late 2007.	13	1
201403	1359	2014-03-19	meeting	ROSENGREN	Because I expect the PCE inflation rate in 2016 to undershoot our 2 percent inflation target by a bit, I have the federal funds rate somewhat lower than the staff assumption at the end of 2016.	13	1

201403	1361	2014-03-19	meeting	ROSENGREN	Such a prolonged miss on inflation and unemployment is one reason for doing all we can to ensure that the return to full employment occurs as quickly as possible.	13	1
201403	1362	2014-03-19	meeting	ROSENGREN	I would note a couple of caveats associated with my forecast.	13	1
201403	1363	2014-03-19	meeting	ROSENGREN	First, I want to thank the staff for doing an incredibly painstaking job estimating the impact of weather on data for the first quarter of this year.	13	1
201403	1364	2014-03-19	meeting	ROSENGREN	My own staff did a careful but somewhat less comprehensive appraisal of the impact of weather on recent data and, like the Board staff, concluded that the effect on this quarter’s real GDP would be rather modest.	13	1
201403	1365	2014-03-19	meeting	ROSENGREN	My estimate of first-quarter real growth, at a bit below 2 percent, implies that growth this quarter likely would have been roughly only 2 percent even in the absence of weather problems.	13	1
201403	1366	2014-03-19	meeting	ROSENGREN	This makes me worry.	13	1

201403	1368	2014-03-19	meeting	ROSENGREN	While I and many others are forecasting such a recovery, tangible support for the forecast has yet to appear in the data, providing ample reason for caution as we consider our policy tomorrow.	13	1

201403	1373	2014-03-19	meeting	ROSENGREN	Furthermore, European banks are much more exposed to Eastern Europe and emerging markets than US banks and are less well positioned to suffer another adverse shock.	13	1

201403	1385	2014-03-19	meeting	ROSENGREN	Furthermore, of those 7.2 million workers who are part time but seeking full-time work, 4.3 million report that the reason they are part-time is because of slack work or business conditions.	13	1
201403	1386	2014-03-19	meeting	ROSENGREN	Note that unlike those that are classified as marginally attached to the workforce, there is no doubt that these people are willing to work since they are already working.	13	1

201403	1388	2014-03-19	meeting	ROSENGREN	Whether one looks at the elevated measures of labor market slack or the continued low readings on compensation and PCE inflation, together they provide ample justification for maintaining a highly accommodative monetary policy for a considerable time.	13	1

201403	1405	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	So far they have not reduced production, but they may need to do so if they don’t see improving sales in the next couple of months.	13	1
201403	1406	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	On the inflation front, cost pressures remain low.	13	1
201403	1407	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	A number of contacts noted falling input prices, such as those for iron ore, steel, and other metals.	13	1
201403	1408	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	This included some businesses with fairly upbeat assessments on demand.	13	1
201403	1409	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	Weakness in China and emerging market economies accounts for some of the reduced demand pressures on materials prices, but a few reports indicated that additional supplier capacity also contributed to reduced materials prices.	13	1
201403	1410	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	Most of those reports were related to iron ore mining.	13	1
201403	1411	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	Of the largest input cost—labor—I heard no concerns about overall wage cost increases.	13	1
201403	1412	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	Sure, some skilled areas continue to be in high demand.	13	1
201403	1413	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	For example, one director cited complaints about shortages of long-haul truck drivers.	13	1
201403	1414	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	Another director in the staffing industry added that drivers quitting for better jobs was something that one often sees in the earliest stages of a labor market rebound.	13	1
201403	1415	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	My CDIAC participants, many of whom are located outside of large metropolitan areas, say it is difficult to find more-skilled workers.	13	1
201403	1416	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	But this really is nothing new.	13	1

201403	1418	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	Frankly, a lot of what I hear from such groups is really about how difficult it always is to run businesses requiring new technologies in smaller regional markets.	13	1
201403	1419	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	Importantly, they don’t appear to be on the leading edge in offering higher wages in order to solve their labor difficulties.	13	1
201403	1420	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	With regard to our national economic outlook, our forecast of GDP growth for this year is down three-tenths since our previous submission, but that is all due to a weaker first quarter.	13	1
201403	1421	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	Looking through the inventory swings and the weather-related slowdown, final demand growth appears to be on a rising trajectory.	13	1
201403	1422	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	So for the balance of the year we still expect growth to average 3 percent or a bit more, with similar numbers in 2015 and 2016.	13	1
201403	1423	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	In our forecast, that kind of growth is enough to eliminate almost all the slack in the economy by the end of 2016.	13	1
201403	1424	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	However, the low inflation outlook remains quite a concern.	13	1
201403	1425	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	The numbers haven’t changed for several meetings.	13	1
201403	1426	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	We are still running far below our target.	13	1
201403	1427	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	My SEP submission does envision an eventual increase in inflation, but we expect it to be slow—only getting to 1¾ percent by the end of 2016.	13	1
201403	1428	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	That’s my forecast, but I have to say that my level of conviction isn’t very high.	13	1
201403	1429	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	While diminishing slack plays some role, my forecast relies heavily on well- anchored inflation expectations pulling actual inflation up toward our 2 percent target.	13	1
201403	1430	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	While we’ve been waiting for this reversion to expectation for quite a while now, it would be useful if we, as a Committee, put a time frame on the table for how long we will allow inflation to seriously underrun our 2 percent PCE objective, much as President Kocherlakota was alluding to when he was asking about what the inflation markers are.	13	1
201403	1431	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	I think it would be very helpful for us to have more of a discussion.	13	1
201403	1432	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	The 2 percent inflation floor presented in alternative A is a useful option if we are serious about defending our inflation objective from below.	13	1
201403	1433	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	For the time being, I am sticking to my view that we will get the expectations updraft and that inflation will move slowly back toward our 2 percent target, but I certainly see downside risks to this forecast.	13	1

201403	1570	2014-03-19	meeting	PIANALTO	The picture is different on the inflation side of the dual mandate.	13	1

201403	1572	2014-03-19	meeting	PIANALTO	While CPI-based measures are higher, research done by my staff suggests that at least part of this difference is attributable to increases in OER inflation, but the ultimate source of these homeownership cost increases is unclear at this point.	13	1
201403	1573	2014-03-19	meeting	PIANALTO	I continue to project that our accommodative monetary policy, a strengthening economy, and stable inflation expectations will bring inflation back toward 2 percent.	13	1
201403	1574	2014-03-19	meeting	PIANALTO	But I expect that the process will be very slow, a view that is shared by the Tealbook.	13	1
201403	1575	2014-03-19	meeting	PIANALTO	Furthermore, my inflation forecasts have tended to creep lower ever so slightly with the passage of time.	13	1
201403	1576	2014-03-19	meeting	PIANALTO	Thus, I continue to see this outlook for inflation as my primary motivation for my position on monetary policy, which I’ll discuss tomorrow.	13	1
201403	1577	2014-03-19	meeting	PIANALTO	In general, I see the risks to my projections as broadly balanced.	13	1
201403	1578	2014-03-19	meeting	PIANALTO	The real economy faces a number of downside risks, including recent geopolitical events and the potential slowdowns in key foreign economies.	13	1
201403	1579	2014-03-19	meeting	PIANALTO	But the economy has also shown considerable resilience over the past year, and as the fiscal drag eases and the labor market improves, upside surprises are certainly plausible.	13	1
201403	1580	2014-03-19	meeting	PIANALTO	At this point these risks to output growth are also the primary risks to my unemployment and inflation projections, resulting in my assessment of risks to both inflation and unemployment as balanced.	13	1
201403	1581	2014-03-19	meeting	PIANALTO	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
