201403	1588	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
201403	1589	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	Economic activity in the Fifth District has continued to improve at a moderate pace since the end of January, but there are clear signs of weakness.	13	1
201403	1590	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	Our business surveys for February point to a contraction in the manufacturing and retail sectors and a modest weakening in the nonretail service sector.	13	1
201403	1591	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	At least some of that weakness was attributable to severe winter weather.	13	1
201403	1592	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	The Board staff provided what I thought was a clever analysis showing which counties were most affected by unusually large snowfalls and unusually cold temperatures, and apparently the Fifth and Sixth Districts were the most heavily affected by the snow relative to seasonal norms.	13	1
201403	1593	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	Weather effects were evident in comments from our contacts in recent weeks.	13	1
201403	1594	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	We heard widespread reports of weather-related delays in construction and a range of construction-supply industries.	13	1
201403	1595	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	Contacts expect activity to catch up in the spring, though, and to add to the typical seasonal pickup in demand.	13	1
201403	1596	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	Manufacturing in the Carolinas was affected by widespread power outages as well as shortages of natural gas due to a weather-related surge in demand.	13	1
201403	1597	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	In addition, many plants shut down just for economic reasons, because the utility costs had gotten so high that it made more sense to shut down for a couple of days.	13	1
201403	1598	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	Some plants lost as many as 10 days during January and February.	13	1
201403	1599	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	Many affected manufacturers were expecting to catch up through overtime and weekend work, and they generally remained optimistic about activity in the spring, as in the Sixth District.	13	1
201403	1600	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	Disruptions were also reported in tourism, shipping, nurseries, fishing, and crabbing.	13	1
201403	1601	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	The storms generated some economic winners, though.	13	1
201403	1602	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	Grocery stores saw their typical flurry of consumers stocking up frantically on necessities, and the chemical industry benefited from a surge in sales of ice melt and windshield washer fluid.	13	1
201403	1603	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	The net effect of the unusual weather is very difficult to quantify, of course.	13	1
201403	1604	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	My staff evidently wasn’t up to the task.	13	1
201403	1605	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	[Laughter]	13	1
201403	1606	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	We will talk about that later, on the way back.	13	1
201403	1607	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	But our information suggests that the weather-related disruptions were large enough to cloud the interpretation of the economic data for our region.	13	1
201403	1608	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	Preliminary results from our March survey, which I got this morning, show a slight decline from February to March for manufacturing and a strengthening on the service side.	13	1
201403	1609	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	The weather wasn’t as bad in March as it was in February.	13	1
201403	1610	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	It does suggest that there’s an underlying theme of weakness on the manufacturing side despite the optimism we’ve heard about.	13	1
201403	1611	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	We’ll have to see as the data come in.	13	1
201403	1612	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	Turning to the national outlook, neither my forecast nor the Tealbook’s has changed much since the previous meeting, apart from the revisions to the fourth and first quarters necessitated by incoming data.	13	1
201403	1613	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	My projection is for GDP growth to continue at about the rate we’ve averaged since the end of the recession.	13	1
201403	1614	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	I again wrote down 2.2 percent.	13	1
201403	1615	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	The Tealbook is projecting faster growth beginning next quarter driven by strong growth in consumption, supported by strong growth in disposable income, which is, in turn, supported by a pickup in productivity growth.	13	1
201403	1616	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	I don’t really see compelling evidence for either of those two supporting factors at this point.	13	1
201403	1617	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	At the previous meeting the case for a strong pickup in growth was bolstered by an apparent surge in real consumer spending in the fourth quarter of last year, but that surge has been largely revised away since then.	13	1
201403	1618	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	For the remainder of my comments I want to say a few words about labor markets.	13	1
201403	1619	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	We’re about to back away, it seems, from forward guidance tied to a specific numerical value of the unemployment rate.	13	1
201403	1620	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	There’s a narrative that has been circulating that says we’re doing this because the unemployment rate has proven to be an unreliable indicator of labor market conditions.	13	1
201403	1621	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	I think that story is unwarranted, and we should discourage that interpretation.	13	1
201403	1622	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	I think we’re dropping the reference to a 6½ percent unemployment rate because the value has been attained, not because the unemployment rate is particularly unreliable now.	13	1
201403	1623	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	And in particular, I think the evidence supports the view that the measured unemployment rate is not terribly distorted or biased right now.	13	1
201403	1624	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	This popular story about why unemployment is unreliable is that there’s a large pool of workers who are classified as out of the labor force because they did not actively look for work in the past four weeks, but who are likely to flood back into the labor market if conditions improve.	13	1
201403	1625	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	In other words, the labor force participation rate is artificially low right now or will rise—or, more realistically, stop falling—when labor market conditions tighten.	13	1
201403	1626	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	In the past I’ve discussed some of the research that staff economists at the Richmond Fed have done related to labor force participation and labor markets.	13	1
201403	1627	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	Some of that work was recently published in our Economic Quarterly, and it’s closely related to this issue.	13	1
201403	1628	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	A paper by Marianna Kudlyak reports on research I mentioned briefly last year.	13	1
201403	1629	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	She estimates a very straightforward model of the impact of demographic change on labor force participation trends.	13	1
201403	1630	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	The model is essentially a parsimonious version of that in the 2006 Brookings Papers article by Aaronson and colleagues that’s cited so often.	13	1
201403	1631	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	It has a set of fixed effects for gender, age, and cohort with an allowance for cyclical variation.	13	1
201403	1632	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	She finds that at the end of 2012, the model prediction for the labor force participation rate is actually a little below the actual labor force participation rate.	13	1
201403	1633	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	In other words, even though the labor force participation rate has been declining in the Great Recession and recovery, all of that decline can now be accounted for by demographic factors, and the cyclical effects have virtually disappeared.	13	1
201403	1634	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	That’s consistent with the pre-recession prediction in that paper by Aaronson and coauthors.	13	1
201403	1635	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	Demographic factors point to a continued decline in labor force participation in her model, as in others, and that suggests we shouldn’t expect the labor force participation rate to flatten out and delay further declines in the unemployment rate.	13	1
201403	1636	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	Another paper in our most recently Economic Quarterly is by Andreas Hornstein, and it looks at the observed negative correlation between cyclical movements in the unemployment rate and the labor force participation rate.	13	1
201403	1637	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	In other words, when the unemployment rate goes up, the labor force participation rate falls.	13	1
201403	1638	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	He finds that the most common interpretation of this correlation, this “discouraged worker” story, seems inconsistent with the underlying gross flows between employment, unemployment, and being out of the labor force.	13	1
201403	1639	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	In the data, a falling unemployment rate is associated with a smaller transition rate from out of the labor force to unemployment, not larger, as the discouraged worker narrative would suggest.	13	1
201403	1640	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	Moreover, a falling unemployment rate is associated with larger transition rates from unemployment to out of the labor force, not smaller, as the discouraged worker narrative would suggest.	13	1
201403	1641	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	So if you think of that story in terms of workers drawn from out of the labor force into unemployment as labor markets improve, it seems inconsistent with the data.	13	1
201403	1642	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	On the other hand, a broader version of that story does seem consistent with the data.	13	1
201403	1643	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	It turns out that a falling unemployment rate is associated with larger transitions from outside the labor force directly into employment, and the significance of those flows from out of the labor force to employment supports the broad concern that measured labor force participation doesn’t properly reflect the number of people who might want to work or are available to work at any given time.	13	1
201403	1644	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	In other words, there isn’t a very sharp distinction between the labor-force attachment of those counted as unemployed and those counted as out of the labor force.	13	1
201403	1645	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	In fact, you can decompose those out of the labor force into different groups based on whether they say they want a job, what search activities they report to have engaged in, or what their reasons are for not wanting a job, and if you do that, you find different transition rates for different groups.	13	1
201403	1646	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	So you can document gradations in the degree of attachment to the labor force among those who aren’t employed, but if you look at those groups in recent years, you don’t see evidence of a disproportionate increase in the size of the subgroups that are more likely to return to the labor force.	13	1
201403	1647	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	In ongoing research, my staff is developing a comprehensive index of the employable labor force that covers both the unemployed and those out of the labor force.	13	1
201403	1648	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	It essentially weights those without a job by their likelihood of transitioning to employment.	13	1
201403	1649	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	Preliminary results suggest that the behavior of this index is currently in line with the behavior of the unemployment rate.	13	1
201403	1650	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	This has been sort of a lengthy discourse.	13	1
201403	1651	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	To sum up, what I take away from this is that a broad range of measures of labor market conditions are providing signals that are essentially in line with the signal provided by the unemployment rate alone.	13	1
201403	1652	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	So I think we should not communicate that the change in our forward guidance reflects an erosion of our confidence in the measured unemployment rate as a guide to policy.	13	1
201403	1653	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	We’ve always emphasized that we look at an array of indicators of labor market conditions.	13	1
201403	1654	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	I think we should continue to do so, but we shouldn’t suggest that there’s an atypically large overhang of discouraged workers relative to normal times.	13	1
201403	1655	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	Thank you.	13	1
201403	1656	2014-03-19	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	13	1
201403	1657	2014-03-19	meeting	YELLEN	President Bullard.	13	1
201403	1658	2014-03-19	meeting	BULLARD	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
201403	1659	2014-03-19	meeting	BULLARD	Before I start, I would just like to say that during the intermeeting period I gave a speech called “The Rise and Fall of Labor Force Participation in the US,” which actually reviewed a lot of the literature that President Lacker just referred to, and I came away from that with a similar conclusion—that the labor force trend in the United States has a reasonable demographic explanation behind it, and that it rose up during the 1970s, ’80s, and ’90s, peaked, and now is on a downward trend.	13	1
201403	1660	2014-03-19	meeting	BULLARD	The models that President Lacker referred to do seem to have predicted, as far back as 2006, that the labor force participation rate would be where it is today.	13	1
201403	1661	2014-03-19	meeting	BULLARD	And so that makes me doubt, along with President Lacker, that there’s a big cyclical component to labor force participation, which there historically has not been in the United States.	13	1
201403	1662	2014-03-19	meeting	BULLARD	I don’t think it exists now, and that makes me think that unemployment is, in fact, a good indicator of the state of labor market performance.	13	1
201403	1663	2014-03-19	meeting	BULLARD	But I would finish by saying that I also agree with President Lacker that we should look at a wide variety of labor market indicators.	13	1

201403	1709	2014-03-19	meeting	GEORGE	As a result, energy firms in the District are seeing more cash flow from natural gas operations, which will likely be used to invest in further drilling for oil and natural gas liquids over the next 6 to 12 months.	13	1

201403	1711	2014-03-19	meeting	GEORGE	WTI crude oil prices benefited from the opening of the Keystone Pipeline’s southern leg, which began shipping crude oil from Cushing, Oklahoma, to refineries in the Gulf Coast on January 22.	13	1
201403	1712	2014-03-19	meeting	GEORGE	This development eased the bottleneck at Cushing, narrowing the spread between WTI and Brent crude oil significantly, from more than $13 per barrel to around $8.	13	1
201403	1713	2014-03-19	meeting	GEORGE	In the agriculture sector, farm income is expected to drop by more than 25 percent in 2014 from last year’s record levels, driven primarily by a sharp drop in expected crop prices.	13	1

201403	1734	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
201403	1735	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I will focus my remarks on the national inflation picture, which remains a matter of great concern to me.	13	1
201403	1736	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	We adopted a 2 percent inflation target in January 2012.	13	1
201403	1737	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Since that time, PCE inflation has averaged 1.3 percent, and the inflation outlook remains subdued.	13	1
201403	1738	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Tealbook A, doesn’t see inflation returning to target until 2018.	13	1
201403	1739	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Under the formulation in Tealbook A of the FOMC’s reaction function, my own inflation outlook is about the same.	13	1
201403	1740	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I should note, yet again, that my SEP outlook has no connection to reality.	13	1
201403	1741	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	[Laughter]	13	1
201403	1742	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	It is grounded in the fundamentally unrealistic assumption that I am in charge of monetary policy.	13	1
201403	1743	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	This inflation outlook—that is, the one based on the reaction function in Tealbook A—is playing a key role in my thinking about policy.	13	1
201403	1744	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	For that reason it’s important to constantly question the validity of the outlook.	13	1
201403	1745	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	With that in mind, in preparation for this meeting I asked my staff this question: What evidence is out there that would suggest that our inflation outlook is misleadingly low?	13	1
201403	1746	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I could go through the long laundry list of data that we examined on statistical forecasts, model-based forecasts, inflation expectations, compensation data, et cetera.	13	1
201403	1747	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Rather, I will just offer you our bottom line.	13	1
201403	1748	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	We found essentially no evidence that suggested a need to raise our baseline outlook for inflation.	13	1
201403	1749	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Indeed, if anything, one could possibly make the case that our inflation outlook and the one that is in Tealbook A, are actually a little optimistic.	13	1
201403	1750	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	This characterization was true nationally, but it is also, and a little more surprisingly, true in the Ninth District economy.	13	1
201403	1751	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	In our District, compensation and pricing pressures remain muted, even though the unemployment rate is now well below 5 percent.	13	1
201403	1752	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The only significant compensation pressures that we see in the Ninth District are in North Dakota, where the unemployment rate has now fallen below 3 percent.	13	1
201403	1753	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I think this inflation performance and this inflation outlook, combined with our recent policy choices, are problematic for the credibility of our 2 percent inflation target.	13	1
201403	1754	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Having an inflation target has two implications.	13	1
201403	1755	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	First, because inflation forecasts are subject to considerable error, successfully targeting 2 percent means that we should typically be facing relatively similar and significant risks of inflation running above or below 2 percent over the medium term.	13	1
201403	1756	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	We don’t right now.	13	1
201403	1757	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	My staff’s review of the evidence strongly suggested that the risks of running below 2 percent over the medium term were larger than the risks of running above 2 percent.	13	1
201403	1758	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	And it’s worth noting that a lot of our public communication continues to suggest that a significant risk of exceeding 2 percent is unwelcome or even unacceptable to the Committee.	13	1
201403	1759	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The second implication of the term “target” is that when we see inflation deviating from 2 percent, we should be purposefully taking steps to redirect the economy toward the level that we find desirable—that is, 2 percent.	13	1
201403	1760	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	We’re not doing that.	13	1
201403	1761	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Instead, we’re content to drift below 2 percent for many years at a time.	13	1
201403	1762	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Indeed, the tapering process could be seen by some as purposely steering the economy away from 2 percent inflation.	13	1
201403	1763	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Now, I’m concerned about low inflation for another reason, and this ties into some of the remarks that Presidents Lockhart and Rosengren made.	13	1
201403	1764	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The FOMC statement refers to persistently low inflation posing a risk to economic performance.	13	1
201403	1765	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I would say that differently.	13	1
201403	1766	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Persistently low inflation signals poor economic performance.	13	1
201403	1767	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	So when we are struggling with the issue that President Bullard and President Lacker raised—the question of how much slack there really is in the labor markets—persistently low inflation is a signal to us about what kind of slack is out there.	13	1
201403	1768	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Having low inflation relative to long-term expectations for inflation suggests that we as a country are leaving key resources underutilized.	13	1
201403	1769	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Now, in my view, we can see that large amount of waste in the data from the labor market.	13	1
201403	1770	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The unemployment rate itself remains high—regardless of what you think of U-3, it still remains high.	13	1
201403	1771	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	But there are other significant markers of this kind of underutilization.	13	1
201403	1772	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The fraction of people who are working part time for economic reasons remains very high by historical standards.	13	1
201403	1773	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	That has been noted by others.	13	1
201403	1774	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	One point that doesn’t get as much attention as I think it deserves is that the fraction of people who have been unemployed less than six months who transition into employment still remains quite low by historical standards.	13	1
201403	1775	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	And this is true even if one focuses on prime-age workers.	13	1
201403	1776	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	What I’m talking about here is the job-finding rate for people who have been unemployed for less than six months.	13	1
201403	1777	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Many have attributed low employment to disability and retirement effects.	13	1
201403	1778	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	But if you look at the fraction of people aged 25 to 54 who are classified as nondisabled by the CPS and who also have a job, that remains very low by pre-recession standards.	13	1
201403	1779	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	That casts some doubt, I would say, on disability and retirement playing the full role in explaining low employment.	13	1
201403	1780	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I see all of these labor market markers as suggesting a large degree of underutilization of American human capital, and that ties together with the low inflation outlook—it is entirely consistent with that perspective.	13	1
201403	1781	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	So putting it another way, by missing our target of 2 percent inflation persistently, we are failing to deliver on the maximum employment part of our mandate as well.	13	1
201403	1782	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I would describe my benchmark outlook as one in which inflation returns to target within the next few years.	13	1
201403	1783	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	But I put more than a little probability on another possible future, in which the inflation rate is around 1½ percent over the next decade.	13	1
201403	1784	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	What would this Committee do in response as this scenario started to unfold or did unfold?	13	1
201403	1785	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I’m highly uncertain about the answer to this question, which means that I’m uncertain about the credibility of the 2 percent inflation target itself in the period ahead.	13	1
201403	1786	2014-03-19	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1

201403	1790	2014-03-19	meeting	DUDLEY	I don’t take much signal from the slowdown in real GDP growth in the current quarter.	13	1

201403	1794	2014-03-19	meeting	DUDLEY	Also, I don’t take much signal from the slowdown in real GDP growth because we did expect some payback from the contributions to GDP from inventories and trade in the current quarter, which were important in boosting the economy in the second half of last year.	13	1
201403	1795	2014-03-19	meeting	DUDLEY	I also think the fact that the payroll employment growth trajectory has held up makes me a bit more optimistic that this is just a short-lived pause.	13	1
201403	1796	2014-03-19	meeting	DUDLEY	To quote that well-known economic prognosticator, Chauncey Gardner, who was the main protagonist of Being There, “There will be growth in the spring.”	13	1
201403	1797	2014-03-19	meeting	DUDLEY	[Laughter]	13	1
201403	1798	2014-03-19	meeting	DUDLEY	That said, I do take some signal from a couple of things.	13	1
201403	1799	2014-03-19	meeting	DUDLEY	The first thing from which I take a signal is the downward revisions to consumer spending that we have seen in the fourth quarter of 2013.	13	1
201403	1800	2014-03-19	meeting	DUDLEY	That does suggest that there is a little bit less impetus for growth from the household sector.	13	1
201403	1801	2014-03-19	meeting	DUDLEY	And I also take some signal from the fact that housing activity seems to have taken a greater hit from the backup in mortgage rates over the past year than I would have expected.	13	1
201403	1802	2014-03-19	meeting	DUDLEY	There is also a broader question that I have been grappling with:  What is the impetus to faster growth in a global context?	13	1
201403	1803	2014-03-19	meeting	DUDLEY	It seems that several EMEs around the world are going to have to move to smaller current account deficits or to current account surpluses, if capital flows reverse.	13	1
201403	1804	2014-03-19	meeting	DUDLEY	China’s growth also seems to be a considerable downside risk.	13	1
201403	1805	2014-03-19	meeting	DUDLEY	We talked about that earlier.	13	1
201403	1806	2014-03-19	meeting	DUDLEY	And so I asked myself the question, what is the impetus on the other side that would push the global economy along at a faster pace?	13	1
201403	1807	2014-03-19	meeting	DUDLEY	With respect to Ukraine, I agree that the direct effects on the US economy are pretty trivial.	13	1
201403	1808	2014-03-19	meeting	DUDLEY	But if it goes a particular way, one in which there is a big standoff between the West and Russia and there are sanctions, I can imagine pretty sizable effects because there could actually be a disruption to commodity supply, and then you would have a significant supply shock to the global economy.	13	1
201403	1809	2014-03-19	meeting	DUDLEY	My bottom line is that I’m more optimistic still than I was a year ago.	13	1
201403	1810	2014-03-19	meeting	DUDLEY	I still expect the economy to rebound to about a 3 percent growth trajectory over the remainder of the year as the fiscal drag abates and consumer spending and business fixed investment are supported by buoyant financial conditions.	13	1
201403	1811	2014-03-19	meeting	DUDLEY	But at the same time, I don’t think a fast recovery is yet “in the bag.”	13	1
201403	1812	2014-03-19	meeting	DUDLEY	I’m worried about the downside risks to my forecast, especially given the fact that our forecast errors have mostly been on that side in recent years.	13	1
201403	1813	2014-03-19	meeting	DUDLEY	On inflation, I agree with President Kocherlakota that the 2 percent objective is supposed to be a target that you hit over time—it’s not a ceiling.	13	1
201403	1814	2014-03-19	meeting	DUDLEY	And so I think we should be trying to spend half the time marginally on both sides of that 2 percent objective.	13	1

201403	1816	2014-03-19	meeting	DUDLEY	Inflation expectations are well anchored.	13	1

201403	1820	2014-03-19	meeting	DUDLEY	So apart from the core inflation readings themselves, the situation doesn’t seem very dire.	13	1

201403	1834	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	I join what seems to me to be something of a consensus around the table that, although developments since the previous FOMC meeting suggest a little diminution in the momentum of the economy, the basic narrative hasn’t changed.	13	1
201403	1835	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	I also agree that a notable development since then has been a significant increase in externally generated downside risks, although, for some of the reasons that Steve explained earlier, it is hard to quantify those, at least at this point.	13	1
201403	1836	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	Like President Lacker, I am going to dedicate most of what I say to labor markets, although probably with a somewhat different direction.	13	1
201403	1837	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	But I do want to begin with a point of agreement with Jeff, which is that in speaking about the anticipated shift in our guidance we should emphasize the fact that we are getting to the 6.5 percent threshold, and of course that is exactly what the language in the proposed paragraph 7 does.	13	1
201403	1838	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	It says, “with the unemployment rate nearing 6½ percent.”	13	1
201403	1839	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	I think it continues to be difficult to reconcile, at least on the basis of past experience and consequent expectations, everything that has been going on in labor markets.	13	1
201403	1840	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	It seems that, for each piece of information suggesting structural change in the labor markets—such as the large number of people out of the labor force who report that they don’t want a job—there is another piece of information suggesting that significant slack remains— such as the continued elevated levels of people who report working part time but who would like a full-time job, which is reflected, of course, in the U-6 number that several people have cited.	13	1
201403	1841	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	We probably are going to continue to have to read most labor market indicators more tentatively than one might have done prior to the recession.	13	1
201403	1842	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	Some, like the US Beveridge curve, have only a relatively short history to begin with.	13	1
201403	1843	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	And even those that have been around a lot longer are likely to be of at least somewhat less assistance in interpreting broader economic trends, precisely because the magnitude of the financial crisis and the Great Recession have either delayed the reversion of behavior by employers and workers to pre-crisis norms, accelerated secular changes in the economy, including labor market dynamism changes that were already at work, introduced a new set of changes in labor market behavior that will persist, or some combination of all three of these possibilities.	13	1
201403	1844	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	There are a number of arguments out there now that purport to establish structural explanations based on experience to date.	13	1
201403	1845	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	In each case, there is, or at some point may be, something to these explanations.	13	1
201403	1846	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	But I don’t think that they can be more than hypotheses right now.	13	1
201403	1847	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	I have to confess to some disappointment that nobody has raised some of those yet today, because I had prepared a stunning rebuttal to any such arguments.	13	1
201403	1848	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	[Laughter]	13	1
201403	1849	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	So I am going to omit several of them—unless one of you has the temerity next time to mention it, in which case I will still be prepared—and instead just talk about one, which is the argument for less slack based on the fact that the rate of short-term unemployment has returned to pre-recession levels.	13	1
201403	1850	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	I don’t think this observation tells us too much beyond the fact that the rate of separations has come down substantially, and that there is hiring going on.	13	1
201403	1851	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	Since the short-term unemployed become, by definition, long-term unemployed after 26 weeks, the declining rate of separations assures that there is going to be a limited universe of the short-term unemployed.	13	1
201403	1852	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	And, more important, it doesn’t really address the issue that should be central for us, which is how much slack does remain in labor markets.	13	1
201403	1853	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	That is, a lot of these things are interesting intellectual questions, and people will be writing about them for years.	13	1
201403	1854	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	But from our point of view, in making monetary policy, the answer to each of these little conundrums is less important than the ultimate question of how much slack does remain.	13	1
201403	1855	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	Now, some have tried to connect the observation on short-term unemployment more directly to slack by arguing either that the long-term unemployed exert little or no downward pressure on wages or that the supposedly stable Beveridge curve relationship between vacancy rates and the short-term unemployed suggests that the long-term unemployed do not represent slack in labor markets.	13	1
201403	1856	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	The Board’s staff circulated a memo last week that addresses the first argument quite well.	13	1
201403	1857	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	On the second, again, I’m not sure the argument adds too much beyond the fact that as a historical matter there are a lot of long-term unemployed relative to short-term unemployed.	13	1
201403	1858	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	And this seems to be the classic case in which it would be a mistake to infer too much from deviations from an observed relationship that was developed over a relatively short time span that did not include anything approaching a disruption in the economy similar to that which we experienced half a decade ago.	13	1
201403	1859	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	Given these unusual circumstances, there could be other factors that are both shifting the Beveridge curve and contributing to long-term unemployment.	13	1
201403	1860	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	Read anything that Peter Diamond has written, and one will come to your mind, which is that in an environment of uncertain demand growth, firms have made less intense efforts to fill some kinds of vacancies or have awaited the perfect candidate rather than filling the vacancy with a serviceable or less experienced one as they might if demand were rising more quickly and the need were more intense.	13	1
201403	1861	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	Going back to the data that we do have, we continue to see that job-finding rates for the long-term unemployed declined during the recession and recovery less than for the short-term unemployed—though of course, in absolute terms, the rates for the former are higher.	13	1
201403	1862	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	It is true that exit rates from the workforce by the long-term unemployed ticked up last year after having remained quite similar to rates for the short-term unemployed throughout most of the recession and recovery, and that is something that might indicate a shift, but to date it is a tentative shift.	13	1
201403	1863	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	I would end by saying, again, that there is almost surely some structural damage here, and it is very unlikely that all of the long-term unemployed represent labor market slack.	13	1
201403	1864	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	But to this point, there really isn’t very convincing evidence for the proposition that they reflect only a very limited amount of slack, which is sometimes the implicit premise of the arguments one hears.	13	1
201403	1865	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	A couple of points on wages.	13	1
201403	1866	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	There has been some evidence of increased wage growth, but I would make two observations.	13	1
201403	1867	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	First, wage growth is hardly generalized or rapid to this point, and I think the anecdotal information that some of you have provided is supportive of that observation.	13	1
201403	1868	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	Second, and maybe more controversially, the prospect that wage gains from what have been very depressed levels for some time will be more generalized and sustained in the next couple of years should quite possibly be read not as bad news—the inevitable sowing of undesirable inflationary seeds—but as good news.	13	1
201403	1869	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	That is, you can make the argument that the economy is in need of a wage recovery that will allocate a larger proportion of productivity gains to labor, thereby reversing recent trends, allowing for more sustained increases in personal consumption expenditures, and thus helping to lay the foundations for a firmer demand, production, and investment cycle.	13	1
201403	1870	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	Uncertainty around what is going on in labor markets makes it an interesting time to be a labor economist, but it makes it somewhat more frustrating for us.	13	1
201403	1871	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	To the degree that this issue has importance for us in deciding on monetary policy, as I indicated a few moments ago, the mixed signals should be of less concern now than they might have been had we experienced a period of rapid growth during the recovery.	13	1
201403	1872	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	Just a couple of years ago, the prevailing view around this table was that eventually we’d see growth rates at an annualized 4 percent or more.	13	1
201403	1873	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	The expectation of rapid growth raised at least the debate as to whether a reasonably rapid tightening in monetary policy might be needed.	13	1
201403	1874	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	But now, probably in part precisely because we have had a modest recovery in place for several years, it seems unlikely that we will experience the sort of spurt in growth that might have engendered concerns about wage pressures and expectations that could get away from us.	13	1

201403	1912	2014-03-19	meeting	STEIN	Another take that I like is, these two guys at Harvard Business School, Sam Hanson and Robin Greenwood, have produced a forecasting model whereby you can predict the future returns over the next two years to holding junk bonds, essentially.	13	1

201403	1955	2014-03-19	meeting	POWELL	The 50 basis point change in our SEP for 2016, in the face of a downbeat paragraph 1 in the statement and market expectations of no change at all, is going to be very hard to explain—more specifically, very hard for you to explain.	13	1
201403	1956	2014-03-19	meeting	POWELL	[Laughter]	13	1

201403	1958	2014-03-19	meeting	POWELL	The first is that we think the economy is better than the market thinks it is.	13	1

201403	1963	2014-03-19	meeting	POWELL	The single worst thing we could do is to send a confusing signal about our reaction function.	13	1

201403	1980	2014-03-19	meeting	POWELL	I want to talk briefly about the question of labor market slack and its implications for the full employment mandate and for the behavior of measures of compensation and broader price inflation.	13	1
201403	1981	2014-03-19	meeting	POWELL	As the economy recovers, these issues are going to be on the front burner, as they obviously already are.	13	1
201403	1982	2014-03-19	meeting	POWELL	The baseline estimate of the natural rate is currently 5.4 percent, which leaves an unemployment gap of about 1.3 percent.	13	1
201403	1983	2014-03-19	meeting	POWELL	To me, there are good reasons to think that labor market slack may be even higher, and I would cite the disproportionate decline of the labor force participation rate below our estimate of trend and the unusually high level of those who are working part time for economic reasons.	13	1
201403	1984	2014-03-19	meeting	POWELL	Labor flows, as Governor Tarullo pointed out—especially quits and hires—suggest a labor market that’s not fully recovered.	13	1
201403	1985	2014-03-19	meeting	POWELL	For these and other reasons, my prior is that the unemployment gap more likely understates slack than overstates it.	13	1
201403	1986	2014-03-19	meeting	POWELL	I also think the level of slack is even more uncertain than usual, so that raises a question:  How do we know?	13	1
201403	1987	2014-03-19	meeting	POWELL	How will one be able to tell that the economy is getting close to full employment?	13	1
201403	1988	2014-03-19	meeting	POWELL	To start to believe that the labor market is getting tight, one would want to see some fairly broad confirmation across a range of labor and other market indicators that we look at, and one particularly important metric would be the growth rate of wage and benefits.	13	1
201403	1989	2014-03-19	meeting	POWELL	As I suggested a moment ago, we care about labor market slack for two closely related but different reasons.	13	1
201403	1990	2014-03-19	meeting	POWELL	First, the full employment mandate directs us to eliminate it.	13	1
201403	1991	2014-03-19	meeting	POWELL	And, second, the inflation mandate would be threatened in either direction if the Committee estimated the gap incorrectly.	13	1
201403	1992	2014-03-19	meeting	POWELL	The excellent memo by Katia Peneva asks whether long-term unemployment exerts less downward pressure on compensation than does short-term unemployment.	13	1
201403	1993	2014-03-19	meeting	POWELL	But, for purposes of the full employment mandate, I’m not sure that matters.	13	1
201403	1994	2014-03-19	meeting	POWELL	Unemployment is unemployment.	13	1
201403	1995	2014-03-19	meeting	POWELL	The fact that some of the unemployed may exert more or less pressure on wages matters for the inflation mandate only.	13	1
201403	1996	2014-03-19	meeting	POWELL	Even as people fall out of the labor force—for example, after their benefits expire—there’s a reasonable likelihood that their attachment to the labor force would return if jobs were plentiful.	13	1
201403	1997	2014-03-19	meeting	POWELL	So policy should not be too eager to give up on these people without some evidence that the labor market is actually getting tight.	13	1
201403	1998	2014-03-19	meeting	POWELL	Moving to wage inflation, the thrust of the memo is that the case advanced by Robert Gordon and others for ignoring long-term unemployment has not been made, and I see the memo as successful in achieving that limited goal.	13	1
201403	1999	2014-03-19	meeting	POWELL	But it also seems to me that risks remain.	13	1
201403	2000	2014-03-19	meeting	POWELL	We know that search intensity declines with the length of unemployment.	13	1
201403	2001	2014-03-19	meeting	POWELL	There’s strong evidence that employers take some signal from the length of unemployment, leading them to prefer candidates unemployed for shorter periods.	13	1
201403	2002	2014-03-19	meeting	POWELL	The rate of job finding declines with the length of unemployment.	13	1
201403	2003	2014-03-19	meeting	POWELL	And, finally, longer-term unemployment is so far above normal levels that we should be cautious about relying on relationships in the data that were stable in more normal periods.	13	1
201403	2004	2014-03-19	meeting	POWELL	With all of that, it seems quite plausible to me that, while Gordon’s case has not yet been made, it may still prove right in the long term.	13	1
201403	2005	2014-03-19	meeting	POWELL	So I would just put this risk under the category of one to be monitored.	13	1
201403	2006	2014-03-19	meeting	POWELL	More broadly and more immediately important, is undesirable compensation inflation a risk in the near term?	13	1
201403	2007	2014-03-19	meeting	POWELL	The evidence suggests to me that it’s not.	13	1
201403	2008	2014-03-19	meeting	POWELL	In a standard model, real compensation should increase with productivity, which has generally run about 1½ percent over the long run and since the crisis.	13	1
201403	2009	2014-03-19	meeting	POWELL	So one might say that the normal level of compensation inflation that is consistent with full employment and price inflation of 2 percent is about 3 to 3½ percent.	13	1

201403	2012	2014-03-19	meeting	POWELL	The fact that this one indicator of wages is beginning to move up is not enough to suggest that the Committee is in danger of falling behind the curve.	13	1

201403	2056	2014-03-19	meeting	YELLEN	You can’t talk to businesses without hearing about the difficulty of hiring certain kinds of labor, and that may have increased somewhat, but that’s probably not a sign the labor market is really tight.	13	1
201403	2057	2014-03-19	meeting	YELLEN	On the second question of whether incoming data on spending and productivity indicate enough strength in the broader economy to support ongoing improvement in the labor market, basically, what I heard in the discussion is that the answer to that question is “yes.”	13	1
201403	2058	2014-03-19	meeting	YELLEN	Obviously, there’s a lot of uncertainty thrown into this assessment by the severe winter weather and by the fact that, in a sense, we got head-faked in January and substantially revised up our outlook.	13	1

201403	2061	2014-03-19	meeting	YELLEN	Obviously, waning fiscal drag provides support.	13	1
201403	2062	2014-03-19	meeting	YELLEN	I noticed that many of you mentioned reports of positive business outlook and business confidence—the view that businesses are feeling good and may be poised to increase their spending.	13	1

201403	2069	2014-03-19	meeting	YELLEN	Most of you seem pretty comfortable with the view—and I think almost all of you have it in your SEP forecasts—that inflation will gradually move back toward 2 percent over time, with diminishing slack and well-anchored inflationary expectations.	13	1

201403	2071	2014-03-19	meeting	YELLEN	We don’t really see evidence at this point that inflation is moving back up over time.	13	1
201403	2072	2014-03-19	meeting	YELLEN	And we have to be very attentive to make sure that that occurs.	13	1
201403	2073	2014-03-19	meeting	YELLEN	I’d summarize by saying that most of you seem to see improvement in labor markets with enough momentum to support ongoing improvement over time; and inflation that’s low and no firm evidence—but a reasonable presumption—that the forces are there to raise it over time.	13	1
201403	2074	2014-03-19	meeting	YELLEN	I’d just add a couple of comments of my own.	13	1
201403	2075	2014-03-19	meeting	YELLEN	I’m not going to add very much.	13	1
201403	2076	2014-03-19	meeting	YELLEN	I agree with those assessments.	13	1
201403	2077	2014-03-19	meeting	YELLEN	I’d say that one thing I do find sobering is that we’ve seen ongoing improvement in labor market conditions in a context in which growth has been running only around 2 percent.	13	1
201403	2078	2014-03-19	meeting	YELLEN	My forecast, like many of yours, shows growth moving up to close to 3 percent or over 3 percent for the next several years.	13	1
201403	2079	2014-03-19	meeting	YELLEN	I think that’s what’s likely to happen.	13	1
201403	2080	2014-03-19	meeting	YELLEN	But, until pretty recently, I thought that that had to happen for us to see continued improvement in the labor market, and I, frankly, no longer think that’s the case.	13	1
201403	2081	2014-03-19	meeting	YELLEN	Since 2010, productivity in the nonfarm business sector has grown at an annual average pace of just 1.1 percent.	13	1
201403	2082	2014-03-19	meeting	YELLEN	The Tealbook has once again revised down potential GDP growth to better reconcile the observed decline in the unemployment rate with GDP growth.	13	1
201403	2083	2014-03-19	meeting	YELLEN	And the Tealbook projects productivity to accelerate noticeably.	13	1
201403	2084	2014-03-19	meeting	YELLEN	I have to say that I’m not so sure, and I think we need to remain open to the possibility that the trend growth rate may have persistently shifted down.	13	1
201403	2085	2014-03-19	meeting	YELLEN	This is something that would be a substantial negative from the perspective of long-run improvement in living standards, but it could well result in a more rapid return to full employment than in the baseline Tealbook forecast.	13	1
201403	2086	2014-03-19	meeting	YELLEN	Finally, on inflation, it seems to me that inflation is now stabilized but at a very uncomfortably low level.	13	1
201403	2087	2014-03-19	meeting	YELLEN	My SEP shows it moving back over time, but I don’t think we fully understand why inflation has been running so low.	13	1
201403	2088	2014-03-19	meeting	YELLEN	And I don’t think that we should take too much comfort from the fact that inflation expectations, whether they’re survey- or market-based measures, seem to be well anchored.	13	1
201403	2089	2014-03-19	meeting	YELLEN	In Japan, inflation has persistently run below the levels that were expected for almost two decades.	13	1
201403	2090	2014-03-19	meeting	YELLEN	A situation in which longer-term nominal rates are boosted by inflation expectations that then run persistently above realized inflation is likely damaging to the economy.	13	1
201403	2091	2014-03-19	meeting	YELLEN	So I will be focused on the behavior of inflation and attentive to the possibility that inflation may not be moving back up to 2 percent in the months ahead.	13	1

201403	2378	2014-03-19	meeting	LOCKHART	I’m also concerned that introducing at this time the subject of the post-liftoff path of the policy rate might encourage an interpretation that our intent is to be less accommodative.	23	2
201403	2379	2014-03-19	meeting	LOCKHART	I would simply append the first sentence of paragraph 6 to the end of paragraph 5 and drop the second sentence.	23	2
201403	2380	2014-03-19	meeting	LOCKHART	Beyond today’s statement, I believe that the Committee would be well advised to think ahead a few meetings and envision how guidance might evolve.	23	2
201403	2381	2014-03-19	meeting	LOCKHART	So let me offer these thoughts.	23	2
201403	2382	2014-03-19	meeting	LOCKHART	As I’ve already commented, I can see the Committee changing the “measured steps” and “not on a preset course” guidance to something more concrete and predictive in the July or September meeting.	23	2
201403	2383	2014-03-19	meeting	LOCKHART	By that time, assuming the economy is conforming to the basic outlook, the Committee should be able to either accelerate the end of purchases or say something to the effect that the asset purchase program will end in October, November, or December, and will be wound down in steps of $10 billion.	23	2
201403	2384	2014-03-19	meeting	LOCKHART	Regarding the “considerable time” guidance, it will be appropriate to drop the reference to “after the asset purchase program ends” once it’s ended.	23	2
201403	2385	2014-03-19	meeting	LOCKHART	At that time, the “considerable time” language could be retained as straightforward qualitative guidance about rate policy.	23	2
201403	2386	2014-03-19	meeting	LOCKHART	I’m satisfied that today’s alternative B, paragraph 5, language is a sufficiently durable framework within which to make necessary adjustments as time passes.	23	2
201403	2387	2014-03-19	meeting	LOCKHART	That is to say, I’m satisfied that the new language does not introduce new elements that might turn out to be awkward or troublesome in future months.	23	2
201403	2388	2014-03-19	meeting	LOCKHART	Regarding paragraph 7, I was okay with the earlier version, and I support the revised version.	23	2
201403	2389	2014-03-19	meeting	LOCKHART	Thank you, Madam Chair.	23	2

201403	2391	2014-03-19	meeting	YELLEN	President Williams.	23	2
201403	2392	2014-03-19	meeting	WILLIAMS	Thank you, Madam Chair.	23	2
201403	2393	2014-03-19	meeting	WILLIAMS	I strongly support alternative B.	23	2
201403	2394	2014-03-19	meeting	WILLIAMS	Data since our last meeting indicate an ongoing gradual recovery and continuing below-target inflation, and this situation calls for maintaining the path for monetary policy that we’ve laid out.	23	2
201403	2395	2014-03-19	meeting	WILLIAMS	So I support a further measured reduction in the pace of asset purchases, along with forward guidance that signals a highly accommodative future path for the funds rate.	23	2
201403	2396	2014-03-19	meeting	WILLIAMS	Alternative B provides this continuity in policy, while successfully navigating the transition from quantitative to qualitative guidance.	23	2
201403	2397	2014-03-19	meeting	WILLIAMS	Importantly, the new qualitative language in paragraph 5 is completely consistent with our past statements about forward guidance.	23	2
201403	2398	2014-03-19	meeting	WILLIAMS	In addition, the wording is robust enough that it can be used for quite some time, under the likely evolution of the economy.	23	2
201403	2399	2014-03-19	meeting	WILLIAMS	Paragraph 6 then provides further clarity about our post-liftoff funds rate expectations.	23	2
201403	2400	2014-03-19	meeting	WILLIAMS	I see it as consistent with the views expressed by the Committee, and, importantly, it does not commit us prematurely to a particular path or tie us to a specific rationale.	23	2
201403	2401	2014-03-19	meeting	WILLIAMS	It also makes clear that this is our current assessment, which might change over time.	23	2
201403	2402	2014-03-19	meeting	WILLIAMS	Paragraph 7 guards against the risk that markets may misinterpret the new statement.	23	2
201403	2403	2014-03-19	meeting	WILLIAMS	Any time we modify language, markets have to infer whether we’re signaling a change in policy or simply responding to events.	23	2
201403	2404	2014-03-19	meeting	WILLIAMS	Paragraph 7 takes that inference problem “off the table” by being explicit about continuity in our policy.	23	2
201403	2405	2014-03-19	meeting	WILLIAMS	In terms of the bracketed language in paragraph 5, in my view, it’s not necessary to be explicit that it’s our “current” assessment.	23	2
201403	2406	2014-03-19	meeting	WILLIAMS	I think that goes without saying.	23	2
201403	2407	2014-03-19	meeting	WILLIAMS	So I’d omit that word, and then, in the second bracketed choice, we can continue to refer to the “current” target range.	23	2
201403	2408	2014-03-19	meeting	WILLIAMS	But I view this as really an editorial distinction, and I’m actually fine with either way.	23	2
201403	2409	2014-03-19	meeting	WILLIAMS	In terms of President Plosser’s comment about the targeted federal funds rate in paragraph 6, I actually hadn’t thought about that carefully before, and I think it’s a good point that, instead of saying “keeping short-term interest rates,” we should say “keeping the targeted federal funds rate.”	23	2
201403	2410	2014-03-19	meeting	WILLIAMS	Now, I recognize that we are going to have a robust and, no doubt, thorough and comprehensive discussion on this issue of what our future policy framework should be.	23	2
201403	2411	2014-03-19	meeting	WILLIAMS	But, at this time, we haven’t had that discussion, and we’ve not expressed to the public what our framework will be.	23	2
201403	2412	2014-03-19	meeting	WILLIAMS	I think that switching to “short-term interest rates” could be a little confusing at this time.	23	2
201403	2413	2014-03-19	meeting	WILLIAMS	If we do decide to go to a framework of not using the federal funds rate as our instrument, then, of course, we can change this language to say “short-term interest rates.”	23	2
201403	2414	2014-03-19	meeting	WILLIAMS	I don’t think that would be confusing.	23	2
201403	2415	2014-03-19	meeting	WILLIAMS	I actually think that there is a potential here for the question “What does this mean?” when, really, what we’re trying to do is to calm down uncertainty at this point.	23	2

201403	2419	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	Thank you, Madam Chair.	23	2
201403	2420	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	I support the $10 billion taper today.	23	2
201403	2421	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	We said it’s a high hurdle for altering that path, and I’m fine with that.	23	2
201403	2422	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	My preference today is to retain our January forward guidance with thresholds in paragraph 5 and maintain this policy language until the unemployment rate falls to 6½ percent or inflation rises to 2½ percent.	23	2
201403	2423	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	This statement language remains fully serviceable even if unemployment falls quickly, owing to our additional guidance that the funds rate will remain at zero well past the time that unemployment falls below 6½ percent.	23	2
201403	2424	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	Since there appears to be perhaps a 50 percent probability that unemployment will remain above 6½ percent at the time of our June meeting, changing this language today as in alternative B prematurely risks an adverse market interpretation, in my opinion.	23	2
201403	2425	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	My biggest concern today is that alternative B, along with the SEP release, looks like a tightening in our monetary policy stance.	23	2
201403	2426	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	This round’s SEP median federal funds rate dot in 2016 is 50 basis points higher than in December.	23	2
201403	2427	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	From yesterday’s discussion, I accept that the dealers and financial markets are prepared for a change in our forward guidance, but I think the current alternative B language is not enough guidance to validate the dealer survey expectations.	23	2
201403	2428	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	The dealer survey implies that markets have priced in a very strong degree of financial accommodation.	23	2
201403	2429	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	For example, the dealers envision a funds rate liftoff in the second half of 2015.	23	2
201403	2430	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	They also expect the funds rate liftoff to occur four quarters after the end of QE3 purchases.	23	2
201403	2431	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	The survey also suggests that the more likely values of the unemployment rate at liftoff are below 6 percent.	23	2
201403	2432	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	These observations seem important to me.	23	2
201403	2433	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	If our statement is as vague as alternative B and financial markets see the higher funds rate projections in the SEP, they may price in tighter financial conditions.	23	2
201403	2434	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	Turning to more-specific elements of alternative B, the qualitative language in the second sentence of paragraph 5 is about as weak as one could formulate for forward guidance.	23	2
201403	2435	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	It repeats that our goals are to support maximum employment and a 2 percent inflation objective, the third sentence says that we will assess a range of labor and inflation data, and that’s pretty much it.	23	2
201403	2436	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	Frankly, this formulation is very much like our June 2011 language before we embarked on stronger forward guidance.	23	2
201403	2437	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	Maybe that’s the choice.	23	2
201403	2438	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	Yes, I can believe that markets are expecting us to change our forward guidance today, but I worry a lot that this guidance is too weak to maintain their expectations, especially given the SEP medians that we’re going to release.	23	2
201403	2439	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	One way to strengthen the forward guidance would be to include the 2 percent inflation floor from alternative A.	23	2
201403	2440	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	In my opinion, this addition would move further toward maintaining our prior stance of accommodation, albeit with a slightly different emphasis on inflation.	23	2
201403	2441	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	In adopting this particular inflation floor following a long period of below-target inflation, the Committee would strongly advance the defense of our inflation objective from below.	23	2
201403	2442	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	This would help remind the public that we have symmetrical preferences for inflation over the long run.	23	2
201403	2443	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	In terms of post-liftoff guidance, I think paragraph 6 helps because it instills a further expectation that any premature policy tightening will most likely proceed only slowly.	23	2
201403	2444	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	Without this paragraph, the risk of a tightening inference by the markets is higher.	23	2
201403	2445	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	But I also think the earlier, fuller Tealbook version of paragraph 7 would have helped a lot.	23	2
201403	2446	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	I realize that many participants feel that our statement is long and unwieldy, and, sure, making efforts to streamline the policy description sounds like a good idea if you retain the most important elements.	23	2
201403	2447	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	But, look, once you’re involved in dressing a pig [laughter], no amount of lipstick is going to change the fact that this is a pig we are working with.	23	2
201403	2448	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	The statement is long and ugly, and it will always, almost surely, be that way.	23	2
201403	2449	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	To maintain the link with our prior policy intentions, it seems to me that the important part of paragraph 7 was repeating explicitly our prior commitment that the funds rate would remain at zero well past the time that the unemployment rate goes below 6½ percent.	23	2
201403	2450	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	This would have been separate from our linkage with the end of asset purchases later this fall.	23	2
201403	2451	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	That additional commentary would prove useful if labor market improvement stalls over the next six months.	23	2
201403	2452	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	A labor market stall, combined with a continuation of 1 percent inflation rates, worries me, and hoping that that doesn’t happen involves accepting more risk.	23	2
201403	2453	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	This meeting is one of those opportunities to meaningfully reinforce that we are pursuing goal-oriented monetary policy to reattain both maximum employment and price stability within a reasonably short period of time.	23	2
201403	2454	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	Madam Chair, as I said at the outset, my preference today is to simply update our January statement for economic developments and the additional $10 billion reduction beginning in April.	23	2
201403	2455	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	In order to strengthen alternative B enough to reduce the risk of an inadvertent policy tightening, two changes could help.	23	2
201403	2456	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	One change is to adopt the 2 percent inflation floor from alternative A.	23	2
201403	2457	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	This choice would show that we’re defending our inflation objective from below.	23	2
201403	2458	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	The other change is to revert to the original Tealbook alternative B language for the fuller version of paragraph 7.	23	2
201403	2459	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	This choice would include a reference to keeping the funds rate at zero well past the time that unemployment falls below 6½ percent.	23	2
201403	2460	2014-03-19	meeting	EVANS	In addition, regarding the commentary about the “short-term interest rates” language in paragraph 6, I agree with the suggestion that mentioning the funds rate instead would be better.	23	2

201403	2465	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	Thank you, Madam Chair.	23	2
201403	2466	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	I support alternative B, although the SEP results do give me some pause, as they did President Evans.	23	2
201403	2467	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	I had thought that, with a set of SEP results that didn’t vary much from last time or from market expectations, the somewhat general language in alternative B would be fine.	23	2
201403	2468	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	And I, on balance, think that’s probably still where we should land.	23	2
201403	2469	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	But there are going to be more difficulties.	23	2
201403	2470	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	To a considerable extent, this is going to fall on the Chair to make the explanations on paragraph 5 and, to some degree, on paragraph 7.	23	2
201403	2471	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	It may be that an explanation or elaboration of paragraph 7 can reinforce the point President Evans made that, at this juncture at least, the guidance we’ve had to date is still operative because we haven’t passed the threshold.	23	2
201403	2472	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	I want to spend most of my comments on paragraph 6, however.	23	2
201403	2473	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	I actually think a case can be made to strengthen paragraph 6.	23	2
201403	2474	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	But, more important, it’s critical that something along the lines of paragraph 6 be included in this statement.	23	2
201403	2475	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	For one thing, without such a provision, we will have identified the factors that will determine when the rate will start to rise, and we’ll have noted some expectations as to how steeply it may rise, but we won’t have identified what we expect the endpoint of that process to be.	23	2
201403	2476	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	Now, one might have said that such a specification would be unnecessary if the shared assumption is that our equilibrium interest rate will be the same as it was supposed to be before the crisis—say, 2 percent above the inflation target, for a total of 4 percent.	23	2
201403	2477	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	But such an assumption would be inconsistent with our own stated expectations.	23	2
201403	2478	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	Imperfect as it is, the SEP does communicate something about our individual and aggregated expectations.	23	2
201403	2479	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	We’ve just been talking about the difficulties the current results may create.	23	2
201403	2480	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	The results presented yesterday show that the central tendency of the Committee’s projections has the economy at or close to both full employment and 2 percent inflation by 2016.	23	2
201403	2481	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	And yet, with a couple of exceptions, everyone on the Committee expects the federal funds rate to be below 4 percent and, in most cases, well below 4 percent.	23	2
201403	2482	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	So that’s something that needs to be explained to markets and to the public.	23	2
201403	2483	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	The explanation has got to be that, individually and in the aggregate, we think there are various reasons why the equilibrium rate in 2016, and for at least some period thereafter, will be lower than 4 percent.	23	2
201403	2484	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	There are lots of reasons, as people have already mentioned, why one might believe this to be the case.	23	2
201403	2485	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	Some, such as incomplete repair of household balance sheets or the effects of fiscal consolidation, can be understood as shocks, or as the lingering consequences of shocks, that will have a restraining effect on growth for a nontrivial but finite time.	23	2
201403	2486	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	In the parlance we’ve been using, these are headwinds.	23	2
201403	2487	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	Other factors—such as demographic change, slowing technological innovation that translates into lower productivity gains, or durable changes in preferences manifested in behavior such as saving rates—are more permanent in nature, though, for that reason, observation of these phenomena would presumably need to continue long enough to convincingly make the case.	23	2
201403	2488	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	There’s another explanation, though, which I think falls somewhere between the persistent but ultimately fading character of headwinds and the enduring character of demographics.	23	2
201403	2489	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	That’s reduction in growth potential.	23	2
201403	2490	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	Most, if not all, of us have, over the past several years, marked down potential GDP growth.	23	2
201403	2491	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	The Tealbook has, on several occasions, explained why the staff has done so as well.	23	2
201403	2492	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	In some sense, of course, this explanation can draw on several other possible explanations, both secular changes and persistent but ultimately finite factors, but it does capture an important set of expectations on our part.	23	2
201403	2493	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	And I’d note that these expectations are also reflected in various survey data and spreads that we see in markets.	23	2
201403	2494	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	Precisely because there seems to be such convergence around this general proposition, even if we differ as to how much potential has decreased, I do think a pretty strong case can be made for the proposition that we don’t need to make the language in paragraph 6 as conditional as it now reads.	23	2
201403	2495	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	Indeed, a case could be made that the levels the Committee views as normal in the longer run are themselves lower than assumed on the basis of pre-crisis experience.	23	2
201403	2496	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	I do note in the SEP that six of us have already indicated that we believe the longer-term rate to be below 4 percent, and I suspect at least a few others on the Committee might come to that conclusion as well.	23	2
201403	2497	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	To be clear, I’m not arguing for such a change in the language in paragraph 6, at least not right now.	23	2
201403	2498	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	We would obviously need to analyze and discuss this issue much more before making such a change.	23	2
201403	2499	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	But I mention the case for it mostly to show that the position reflected in the proposed language is actually a good deal softer than the position that might be derived from the Committee’s own projections and stated assessments of growth potential.	23	2
201403	2500	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	I don’t disagree with those who have observed that the current language states the proposition without explaining why—President Plosser made this point today, and I think President Fisher made it in the teleconference meeting.	23	2
201403	2501	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	I’ve just explained why I think we could give such an explanation based on what Committee members currently believe.	23	2
201403	2502	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	But my more immediate response is that omission of any particular reason allows a certain optionality for the Committee.	23	2
201403	2503	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	If you think headwinds alone justify such guidance, your position is preserved, even though someone else may feel that the reasons are likely to have greater permanence.	23	2
201403	2504	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	While this may not be ideal, it would be far worse to leave markets and the public with no indication that the Committee has even recognized the issue and with no sense of where the upward trajectory of the federal funds rate would end.	23	2
201403	2505	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	I will end by saying I agree with others that Charlie Plosser makes a good point about the federal funds rate as opposed to short-term interest rates.	23	2
201403	2506	2014-03-19	meeting	TARULLO	Thank you, Madam Chair.	23	2

201403	2509	2014-03-19	meeting	PIANALTO	Thank you, Madam Chair.	23	2
201403	2510	2014-03-19	meeting	PIANALTO	With the economy continuing to recover and the outlook little changed since the January meeting, I believe it’s clearly appropriate to continue to slow the pace of asset purchases as laid out in alternative B.	23	2
201403	2511	2014-03-19	meeting	PIANALTO	That’s the easy part of today’s decision.	23	2
201403	2512	2014-03-19	meeting	PIANALTO	Changing our forward guidance on the fed funds rate is the more challenging issue.	23	2
201403	2513	2014-03-19	meeting	PIANALTO	We face a tradeoff between maximizing clarity and maintaining flexibility in our guidance.	23	2
201403	2514	2014-03-19	meeting	PIANALTO	I preferred providing more, rather than less, clarity in our guidance.	23	2
201403	2515	2014-03-19	meeting	PIANALTO	In particular, as I mentioned in the videoconference, I preferred forward guidance that uses an inflation floor, for three reasons.	23	2
201403	2516	2014-03-19	meeting	PIANALTO	First, inflation forecasts can capture many of the factors that are likely to determine liftoff but which are hard to spell out simply in forward guidance.	23	2
201403	2517	2014-03-19	meeting	PIANALTO	Second, an inflation floor would refer to the component of our dual mandate for which we have a quantitative goal.	23	2
201403	2518	2014-03-19	meeting	PIANALTO	And, third, as liftoff draws nearer, the Committee will probably need to provide the public with further clarifications of its intentions in order to manage interest rate expectations.	23	2
201403	2519	2014-03-19	meeting	PIANALTO	All of that being said, I recognize that a majority of the Committee does not support including forward guidance with an inflation floor.	23	2
201403	2520	2014-03-19	meeting	PIANALTO	Many on the Committee place a premium on maintaining flexibility about which conditions will warrant liftoff of the fed funds rate and when.	23	2
201403	2521	2014-03-19	meeting	PIANALTO	I appreciate that flexibility does have its benefits, and I have argued for flexibility in other situations.	23	2
201403	2522	2014-03-19	meeting	PIANALTO	The more qualitative forward guidance that’s laid out in the current alternative B has evolved and has improved considerably since the first draft that we looked at.	23	2
201403	2523	2014-03-19	meeting	PIANALTO	So today I do support alternative B.	23	2
201403	2524	2014-03-19	meeting	PIANALTO	Like President Plosser and others who have already commented, I do have a strong preference for referring to the federal funds rate in paragraph 6 rather than short-term interest rates.	23	2
201403	2525	2014-03-19	meeting	PIANALTO	At this point, it isn’t clear what the future operating framework will be, and referring to short-term interest rates could create confusion among the public and distract from the main message of the statement today.	23	2
201403	2526	2014-03-19	meeting	PIANALTO	And, obviously, the SEP also provides projections for the federal funds rate rather than other short-term interest rates.	23	2
201403	2527	2014-03-19	meeting	PIANALTO	Thank you, Madam Chair.	23	2

201403	2541	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	Does paragraph 6 convey new information or not?	23	2
201403	2542	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	Why did we add it now when, in the next paragraph, we’re emphasizing that our forward guidance is the same?	23	2
201403	2543	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	Second, more broadly, I think it’s ambiguous and treads on ground that the Committee has yet to really thoroughly survey and understand.	23	2
201403	2544	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	What time period does this refer to?	23	2
201403	2545	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	You look at the language that says “mandate-consistent levels.”	23	2
201403	2546	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	You look at the SEP for 2016, and it looks as though it lines up near there.	23	2
201403	2547	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	But, on the other hand, paragraph 6 seems to refer to something beyond 2016 as well.	23	2
201403	2548	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	It says “even after” we get to near mandate-consistent levels.	23	2
201403	2549	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	I’d point out that, of course, we haven’t said what mandate-consistent levels are for 2016.	23	2
201403	2550	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	We have the longer-run projections.	23	2
201403	2551	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	We haven’t defined that as congruent with “mandate consistent.”	23	2
201403	2552	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	We never said that.	23	2
201403	2553	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	The January 2012 statement explicitly avoids equating the longer-run values with mandate-consistent levels.	23	2
201403	2554	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	So we’re muddying up that nuance there.	23	2
201403	2555	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	Now, I know there’s been discussion, to which Governor Tarullo has alluded, about the possibility that the appropriate intercept term in a Taylor rule—and some people refer to it as the equilibrium real rate—might have fallen.	23	2
201403	2556	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	But it was just a stray comment or two at the last meeting, and I’m not sure we’ve really digested the possibility that that’s going to be the case.	23	2
201403	2557	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	Besides, a whole lot of us still have the funds rate in the longer run at 4 percent.	23	2
201403	2558	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	So I don’t think we know enough now, on a sustained basis, about what the trajectory after 2016 is going to look like.	23	2
201403	2559	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	I just don’t think we’ve done our homework to be able to put in something of this specificity about the path after the liftoff.	23	2
201403	2560	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	For those two reasons, the statement would be much better without the second sentence in paragraph 6.	23	2
201403	2561	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	And I share President Plosser’s concern about “short-term interest rates.”	23	2
201403	2562	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	I think this would be new to speak about our keeping short-term interest rates, as opposed to the funds rate, at any particular level.	23	2
201403	2563	2014-03-19	meeting	LACKER	But, otherwise, I support alternative B.	23	2
